Kitabah on Wine: Valid Among Disbelievers, Shifts to Value Upon Conversion
Ruling on kitabah between disbelievers for wine and effect of conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a kitabah contract for wine between disbelievers, identify when the consideration shifts to value upon conversion, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether payment of the original wine after conversion frees the slave.

Core Idea
A kitabah contract for wine, valid among disbelievers, shifts to value upon one party's conversion to Islam.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a nuanced scenario: a disbelieving master contracts kitabah with his disbelieving slave for wine or pig, and then one of them embraces Islam. The contract is initially valid because wine is considered mal mutaqawwim (valuable property) for disbelievers. But Islam changes the legal status: the Muslim cannot own or transact wine, so the consideration necessarily shifts to its value (qimah). The passage also records an important intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf about whether paying the wine after conversion can still free the slave, with distinguished scholars on both sides.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial validity of kitabah for wine
Recognize that among disbelievers, wine is mal mutaqawwim, so the kitabah contract is valid (sahih) if the wine is specified or described. This is the Hanafi position, contrary to other schools.
The passage explicitly states: 'يصح من الخمر معيناً'.
2
Analyze the effect of conversion on the consideration
When either the master or the slave becomes Muslim before delivery of the wine, the contract shifts from the wine to its monetary value (qimah), because a Muslim cannot legally take ownership of wine. Exception: if the wine was specific (mu'ayyan), ownership transfers immediately at contract, and conversion does not shift to value.
The value stands in place of the wine, and the slave must pay that value to be freed.
3
Distinguish between specific and non-specific wine
If the wine is non-specific (ghayr mu'ayyan), the contract was upon a description, and conversion shifts to value. If the wine is specific, the slave already owns it in the sense that delivery is only transfer of possession; conversion does not change the consideration.
The same rule applies to pig: specific pig remains; non-specific pig shifts to the value of the slave himself.
4
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on payment of wine after conversion
Some mashayikh (e.g., al-Sarakhsi, al-Nisaburi) hold that paying the wine after conversion does not free the slave; only paying the value does. Others (in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir, etc.) hold that paying the wine also frees him. Compare the reasoning: the former argue that the contract has shifted to value; the latter analogize to the case of a Muslim master's invalid kitabah, where paying wine frees.
The passage does not give a decisive tarjih; later practice tends to require value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( أو كاتب كافر عبده الكافر على خمر) يعني يصح من الخمر معيناً لأن الخمر عندهم مال متقوم كالعصير في حق العقد للآخر إذا سمى قدراً . المسلم فيصح تسميته إذا كان معلوماً. واحترز بقوله «عبده الكافر عن عبده المسلم فإنه يقع فاسداً وتجب القيمة على ما بينا فيما إذا كان المولى مسلماً أطلق في الكلام فشمل الذمي والمستأمن والحربي، ولا فرق في الذمي بين أن يكون في دارنا أو في دار الحرب حيث دخل غير مهاجر لأنه من أهل دارنا فتجري عليه أحكامنا والمستأمن ما دام في دارنا تجري عليه أحكامنا. وإنما محل النظر لو كاتب الحربي عبده المسلم في دار الحرب على خمر أو خنزير فأدى ذلك فالظاهر أنه يعتق أخذاً من قولهم لنا أن نحتال على مال الحربي بأي وجه كان هذا يرضاه، ولا يخفى أن الخنزير هنا كالخمر في الحكم فيه . قال رحمه الله : (وأي أسلم فله قيمة الخمر) لأن المسلم ممنوع عن تمليك الخمر وتملكه وفي تسليم عين الخمر تمليكها وتملكها إذا كان المولى يملكها قبل التسليم لكونها موصوفة في الذمة والقبض يرد على معين فيكون غير ما ورد عليه العقد فيكون تمليكاً العبد وتملكاً . من من المولى في الحال عوضاً عما في الذمة فلا يجوز في حق المسلم فعجز عن تسليم الخمر فوجب المصير إلى القيمة لقيامها مقام المسمى والكتابة باقية على حالها بخلاف ما إذا باع ذمي من ذمي بخمر ثم أسلم أحدهما قبل القبض حيث يفسد البيع عند البعض لأن العقد يقع على ما يصلح بدلاً ففي الكتابة تصلح القيمة بدلاً فيما إذا كاتبه على وصف أو نحوه، ولهذا يجبر المولى على قبول القيمة والبيع لا ينعقد على القيمة صحيحاً أصلاً فكذا لا يبقى عليها قيدنا أصل المسألة بأن الخمر غير معين لأنه لو كان الخمر معيناً فقد ملكه بمجرد العقد والتسليم نقل من يد إلى يد والمسلم غير ممنوع من وضع يده على الخمر، ألا ترى أن المسلم إذا غصب خمراً من ذمي فأسلم الذمي فله أن الخمر من الغاصب ولا يمنع منه في هذه الحالة فإذا أسلم لا ينتقل إلى القيمة وله الخمر لا غير. قيد المسألة بالخمر ولم يتعرض للخنزير فنقول : لو كاتبه على خنزير معين ملكه العقد فإذا أسلم أحدهما قبل القبض لا ينتقل إلى القيمة بل له الخنزير المعين والمسلم لا يمنع من وضع يده عليه كما لو غصب الذمي خنزيراً فأسلم فله أن يرده من يد الغاصب، فلو كان الخنزير غير معين فأسلم أحدهما ينتقل إلى قيمة نفس المكاتب أخذاً من قولهم قيمة القيمي يسترد بمجرد تقوم مقام عينه، وهذا من خواص هذا الكتاب والحمد لله الذي هدانا لهذا قال رحمه الله . (وعتق بقبضها) يعني يعتق بقبض قيمة الخمر لأن الكتابة عقد معاوضة وسلامة أحد العوضين لأحدهما توجب سلامة العوض للآخر، وإذا أدى الخمر عتق أيضاً لتضمن الكتابة تعلق بأداء الخمر كما إذا كاتب المسلم عبده على خمر كما تقدم . قال . في الكافي : : هذا ذكره بعض المشايخ كالقاضي ظهير الدين الشيرازي ونجم الدين الأفطسي والسرخسي والنيسابوري وفي شرح الجامع الصغير وفي شرح الطحاوي والتمرتاشي : ولو أدى الخمر لا يعتق، ولو أدى القيمة يعتق لأن الكتابة انتقلت إلى القيمة ولم يبق الخمر بدلاً في هذا العقد لأن العقد صحيحاً وقع صحيحاً على الخمر ابتداء وبقي بعد الإسلام على قيمته صحيحاً على حاله فخرج الخمر عن كونه بدلاً فيه ضرورة، وبأداء غير البدل لا يعتق بخلاف مسألة المسلم حيث يعتق بأداء الخمر لأن العقد فيه انعقد فاسداً فيعتق بأداء البدل المشروط لما فيه من معنى التعليق ويضمن لمولاه قيمة نفسه وقد تقدم فرق آخر. وفرق في النهاية بفرق ثالث حيث قال : فإن قلت : ما الفرق بين هذا وبين ما إذا كاتب المسلم عبده إلى الخمر ابتداء حيث يعتق العبد بأداء الخمر وإن وقع العقد فاسداً وفيما نحن فيه هو ما إذا كاتب النصراني عبده الكافر على خمر ثم أسلم أحدهما ثم أدى الخمر لا يعتق مع أن القياس ينبغي أن يعتق بأداء الخمر بالطريق الأولى
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (Or a disbeliever makes a kitabah contract with his disbelieving slave for wine) meaning it is valid for specific wine because wine is considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) among them, like grape juice, in the context of a contract for the other if he mentions a quantity. For a Muslim, its specification is valid if it is known. And he restricted with his statement 'his disbelieving slave' regarding his Muslim slave, for it would be invalid and the value becomes obligatory, as we explained earlier. When the master is Muslim, the statement is general and includes the dhimmi, musta'min, and harbi. There is no difference for the dhimmi whether he is in our abode or in dar al-harb as long as he entered as a non-muhajir, because he is from the people of our abode, so our rulings apply to him. And the musta'min, as long as he is in our abode, our rulings apply to him. The only point of consideration is if a harbi makes kitabah with his Muslim slave in dar al-harb for wine or pig, and he pays that, then apparently he is freed, taking from their statement that we may use any means to take the property of a harbi that he agrees to. And it is not hidden that the pig here is like wine in its ruling. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And whichever of them becomes Muslim, he gets the value of the wine) because a Muslim is prevented from owning and possessing wine. In delivering the specific wine, it would be giving ownership and possession if the master owned it before delivery, because it was specified in the dhimma, and receipt is for a specific item, so it becomes other than what the contract was upon, and thus the slave would own it and the master would own it as an immediate consideration for what was in the dhimma. That is not permissible for a Muslim, so he is unable to deliver the wine, so recourse to value is required because it stands in place of the named item, and the kitabah remains as it was. Unlike if a dhimmi sold to a dhimmi wine and then one of them became Muslim before delivery, where the sale becomes corrupt according to some, because the contract is for something suitable as consideration. In kitabah, value is suitable as consideration when he contracted for a description or the like, and therefore the master is compelled to accept the value. A sale is not valid on value at all, so it does not remain on it. Our qualification of the original issue is that the wine is non-specific, because if the wine were specific, he would own it by the contract alone, and delivery transfers from hand to hand, and a Muslim is not prevented from placing his hand on wine. Do you not see that if a Muslim unlawfully took wine from a dhimmi and then the dhimmi became Muslim, he may take the wine from the usurper and is not prevented from it in this case? So when he becomes Muslim, he does not shift to value; he gets the wine only. He restricted the issue to wine and did not address pig. We say: If he made kitabah for a specific pig, he owns it by the contract; then if one of them becomes Muslim before delivery, he does not shift to value but gets the specific pig, and the Muslim is not prevented from placing his hand on it, as if a dhimmi usurped a pig and then became Muslim, he may recover it from the usurper. If the pig is non-specific and one of them becomes Muslim, he shifts to the value of the mukatab himself, taking from their statement that the value of a valuable item (qimi) when recovered stands in place of its specific item. This is among the special features of this book. Praise be to Allah who guided us to this. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And he is freed by receiving it) meaning he is freed by receiving the value of the wine, because kitabah is a contract of exchange, and the safety of one of the two considerations for one party necessitates the safety of the consideration for the other. And if he pays the wine, he is also freed, because the kitabah includes attachment to the payment of the wine, as if a Muslim made kitabah with his slave for wine, as preceded. He said in al-Kafi: This was mentioned by some mashayikh like al-Qadi Zahir al-Din al-Shirazi, Najm al-Din al-Afriti, al-Sarakhsi, and al-Nisaburi. And in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir, Sharh al-Tahawi, and al-Tamartashi: If he pays the wine, he is not freed; if he pays the value, he is freed, because the kitabah has shifted to the value and the wine no longer remains as a consideration in this contract, because the contract was validly concluded on the wine initially and remained after Islam on its value validly, so the wine necessarily left being a consideration. Payment of something other than the consideration does not free him. Unlike the case of the Muslim where he is freed by paying the wine, because the contract there was concluded invalidly, so he is freed by paying the stipulated consideration due to the element of ta'liq (suspension). And he guarantees to his master the value of himself. Another distinction has preceded. And a third distinction is mentioned in al-Nihaya where he said: If you ask: What is the difference between this and the case where a Muslim makes kitabah with his slave for wine from the beginning, where the slave is freed by paying the wine even though the contract was invalid? In our case, it is that a Nasrani made kitabah with his disbelieving slave for wine, then one of them became Muslim, then he paid the wine – he is not freed, even though analogy would suggest he should be freed by paying the wine, a fortiori.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage deals with the complex scenario of a disbelieving master contracting kitabah with his disbelieving slave for wine or pig, and then one of them converts to Islam. The commentator explains that the initial contract is valid among disbelievers because wine is considered valuable property in their laws. However, if the master is Muslim, the contract would be invalid and the slave must pay the value. The key issue is the effect of conversion. If one party becomes Muslim before the wine is delivered, the contract shifts from wine to its value (qimah), because a Muslim cannot legally own or transact wine. The slave becomes free upon paying the value. The commentator also distinguishes between specific and non-specific wine: if the wine was specified (mu'ayyan), ownership transfers immediately upon contract, and conversion does not shift to value; the slave still pays the wine, and the Muslim can hold it. For pig, similar rules apply: if specific, no shift; if non-specific, the slave pays the value of himself (qimat nafsihi). The passage then records a significant intra-Hanafi dispute about whether the slave is freed by paying the original wine after conversion. Some mashayikh (e.g., al-Sarakhsi, al-Nisaburi) hold that paying the wine does not free him, only paying the value does, because the contract after conversion is on the value. Others (in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir, etc.) hold that paying the wine also frees him, by analogy with a Muslim master's invalid kitabah. The commentator presents multiple distinctions, including the fact that in the Muslim's case the contract was invalid from the start, whereas here it was valid initially. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated, but the presentation leans toward the view that payment of value is required.

Key Terms
الخمر (al-khamr): Wine, considered mal mutaqawwim (valuable property) for disbelievers but not for Muslims
القيمة (al-qimah): Monetary value that stands in place of the named consideration when the original becomes impermissible
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspension of a legal effect upon occurrence of a condition, here referring to the kitabah contract being tied to payment
معين (mu'ayyan): Specific, identified; in contrast to non-specific (ghayr mu'ayyan) described in the dhimma

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a kitabah contract between a disbelieving master and his disbelieving slave for wine, what happens if the master becomes Muslim before delivery?
A) The contract becomes void and the slave goes free without payment.
B) The contract shifts to the value of the wine, and the slave must pay that to be freed.
C) The contract remains valid on the wine and the master can still receive it.
D) The slave is freed immediately without payment.

Key Takeaway
A kitabah contract for wine between disbelievers is valid; upon conversion, the consideration shifts to value unless the wine is specific, and there is a legitimate ikhtilaf on whether paying the wine after conversion frees the slave.

Invalid Conditions in Kitaba: Core vs. Periphery
Ruling with proof: Invalid conditions in kitaba outside the core do not invalidate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that invalid conditions in a kitaba contract do not invalidate it unless they affect the substitute (core) and apply this distinction to given scenarios.

Core Idea
Invalid conditions in kitaba are harmless unless they touch the substitute — the soul of the contract.

Not every invalid condition destroys a contract. In the case of kitaba (a slave's contract to earn freedom), the Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between conditions that affect the core (the substitute) and those that are peripheral. This lesson unpacks that distinction, the analogical reasoning behind it, and a refined definition of 'core' from later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
What is the act? Kitaba contract. What is the default ruling when invalid conditions are attached? It remains valid (sahih) unless the condition is in the core.
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Locate the exception
When does an invalid condition invalidate kitaba? When it is in the core (ṣulb al-ʿaqd), specifically in the substitute (badal). Examples: stipulating kitaba for wine or pork, or requiring additional service after freedom.
The core is limited to the substitute and its counterpart (freedom).
3
Understand the proof
The author cites Quran 73:20 to show that travel for earning is permissible, implying the validity of kitaba despite invalid conditions.
The verse is used as evidence that the purpose of kitaba (earning) is valid, so conditions not affecting that purpose do not invalidate.
4
Examine the analogical reasoning
Kitaba resembles sale (invalidated by core conditions) and marriage (not invalidated by conditions outside core). Because after full payment, rescission is not possible (like marriage), only core conditions matter.
The dual analogy is key to understanding why the school limits invalidation to core conditions.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi refinement
Al-Atabiyya argued that conditions corresponding to the substitute (e.g., restricting the slave's movement) are also in the core. The author rejects this: being a specification of the core does not mean it is inside the core.
The majority position (and mufta bihi) restricts the core to the substitute itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المكاتب باب ما يجوز للمكاتب أن يفعله وما لا يجوز من اليد على وجه الاستمداد والاختصاص بنفسه ومنافع نفسه واكتسابه وأن لا يتحكم عليه أحد ويحصل المال بأي وجه شاء فكانت هذه الشروط باطلة والسفر مظنة تحصيل المال . قال الله تعالى وآخرون يضربون في الأرض يبتغون من فضل الله ﴾ [المزمل: ٢٠] والكتابة لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة كما تقدم إلا إذا كان داخلاً في صلب العقد وهو أن يكون في البدل مثل أن يشترط خدمته أو مكاتبته على خمر أو خنزير فيفسد العقد لأن الكتابة تشبه البيع من حيث إنها تحتمل الفسخ قبل أداء البدل فيفسد العقد إذا وجد الشرط في صلب العقد، وتشبه النكاح من حيث إنها لا تحتمل الفسخ بعد الأداء لأنها مبادلة مال بمال في حق المولى ومبادلة مال بغير مال في حق العبد لأنه لا يملك نفسه فلا يفسد العقد بالشروط إذ لم يكن في صلب العقد كما هنا. قال في العتابية : والتمكن في صلب العقد أن هو يدخل في أحد البدلين والذي ليس في صلب العقد هو الذي ليس في بدل الكتابة ولا فيما يقابله، وقد رد عليه بعض العلماء بأن قوله ولا فيما يقابله» ممنوع فإن مقابلة فك الحجر وحرمة المنع من الخروج تخصيص للفك والحرية فتأمل. أقول : ليس ذلك بشيء لأن كون المنع من الخروج تخصيصاً للفك والحرية لا يقتضي كونه داخلاً فيها فإن تخصيص الشيء قد يكون بأمر خارج عنه أخص منه كما إذا عرفنا الإنسان بالحيوان الضاحك فتأمل ا هـ . رحمه الله :
Book of Mukatab: Chapter on what is permissible for the mukatab to do and what is not. From the perspective of seeking support and being unique to himself, his benefits, his earnings, and that no one controls him, and he acquires wealth in any way he wishes, so these conditions are invalid. Travel is a means of acquiring wealth. Allah Almighty said: 'And others traveling in the land seeking the bounty of Allah' [Al-Muzzammil: 20]. Kitaba is not invalidated by invalid conditions as previously mentioned, unless they are inside the core of the contract, meaning they are in the substitute, such as stipulating his service or writing kitaba for wine or pork, then the contract becomes invalid because kitaba resembles sale in that it admits rescission before payment of the substitute, so the contract is invalid if the condition is in the core of the contract. And it resembles marriage in that it does not admit rescission after payment, because it is an exchange of property for property from the master's side and an exchange of property for non-property from the slave's side, since he does not own himself, so the contract is not invalidated by conditions when they are not in the core of the contract, as here. Al-Atabiyya said: 'Being in the core of the contract means it enters into one of the two substitutes, and that which is not in the core is that which is not in the substitute of kitaba nor in what corresponds to it.' Some scholars objected to him by saying his statement 'nor in what corresponds to it' is rejected, for the correspondents of the lifting of restriction and the prohibition of going out constitute a specification of lifting and freedom, so reflect. I say: That is nothing, for the fact that the prohibition of going out is a specification of lifting and freedom does not necessitate that it is inside them, for specifying something may be by something external that is more specific than it, as when we define man as the laughing animal, so reflect. End. May Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the commentary explaining that invalid conditions in the contract of kitaba do not render the contract invalid unless they are embedded in the core of the contract, specifically in the substitute (badal). The author begins by listing invalid conditions that are void, then cites a Quranic verse to show that travel for earning is permissible, implying the validity of the contract. He then draws two analogies: kitaba resembles sale (which is invalidated by core conditions) and resembles marriage (which is not invalidated by conditions outside the core). The passage includes a quote from al-Atabiyya on defining what is in the core, and a rebuttal clarifying that being outside does not mean it is inside. The overall message is that the Hanafi school upholds the validity of kitaba despite invalid conditions unless they directly affect the substitute.

Key Terms
الكتابة (al-kitāba): A contract between a master and his slave whereby the slave is required to pay a specified amount in installments in exchange for freedom upon full payment.
صلب العقد (ṣulb al-ʿaqd): The core or essence of the contract, specifically the substitute (badal) in the contract of kitaba.
شرط فاسد (sharṭ fāsid): An invalid condition that is not legally recognized and does not affect the validity of the contract if it is outside the core.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does an invalid condition invalidate the contract of kitaba?
A) Any invalid condition, regardless of its place.
B) Only when it is in the core of the contract, specifically in the substitute.
C) Only when it involves a prohibition like wine or pork.
D) Invalid conditions never invalidate the contract.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a kitaba contract hinges on the soundness of its core (substitute); invalid conditions elsewhere are harmless.

Mukatab's Capacity to Write Kitaba for His Slave
Ruling that a mukatab may write kitaba for his own slave and related wala' rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mukatab contracting kitaba with his own slave, identify the condition for wala' transfer, and explain the additional restrictions on marriage and gift.

Core Idea
A mukatab may write kitaba for his own slave; wala' goes to him only if the slave pays after his freedom.

In Hanafi fiqh, a mukatab (a slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom) enjoys certain capacities despite remaining partially enslaved. One such capacity is the ability to enter into a kitaba contract with his own slave. This short passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq states the ruling and clarifies crucial conditions: the wala' (patronage) of the sub-slave belongs to the mukatab only if payment occurs after his emancipation. It also touches on the mukatab's limited authority in marriage and gift-giving.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and the act
The passage begins with 'وكتابة عبده' – 'and the kitaba of his slave'. Determine who 'his' refers to (the mukatab) and what act is being permitted.
2
Analyze the conditional wala' rule
The phrase 'الولاء له إن أدى بعد عتقه وإلا لسيده' establishes a condition: if the slave pays after the mukatab becomes free, wala' goes to the mukatab; otherwise to the original master. Understand why this distinction matters.
Wala' is a legal relationship of inheritance and responsibility.
3
Note the additional rulings on marriage and gift
The passage adds two more rulings: the mukatab may not marry without permission, but his gift to others is valid. Recognize that these show the mukatab's partial capacity – he owns property but not full personal autonomy.
4
Summarize the overall capacity of a mukatab
Synthesize: the mukatab can own slaves and contract kitaba, can give gifts, but cannot marry without master's permission. This is consistent with his intermediate status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكتابة عبده والولاء له إن أدى بعد عتقه وإلا لسيده لا التزوج بلا إذن والهبة لغيره يصح
And the kitaba of his slave, and the wala' is for him if (the slave) pays after his (the mukatab's) emancipation, otherwise it is for his master. He may not marry without permission, and his gift to others is valid.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This is a concise matn statement from the book of kitaba. It states that a mukatab (slave under kitaba) has the capacity to enter into a kitaba contract with his own slave (i.e., his slave). If the slave pays the kitaba after the mukatab becomes free, then the wala' (patronage) goes to the former mukatab. If the slave pays before the mukatab's freedom, or if the mukatab remains a slave, the wala' goes to the original master. Additionally, the mukatab cannot marry without his master's permission, but his gift (hiba) to others is valid. These are established Hanafi rulings concerning the capacities of a mukatab.

Key Terms
كتابة العبد (kitābat al-ʿabd): The act of a mukatab making his own slave a mukatab, i.e., contracting a kitaba with his slave.
الولاء (al-walāʾ): Patronage relationship that arises between the emancipator and the freed slave, involving inheritance and responsibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, when does the wala' of a sub-slave go to the mukatab?
A) When the sub-slave pays the kitaba at any time.
B) When the sub-slave pays after the mukatab becomes free.
C) When the mukatab's master consents.
D) Always, because the mukatab wrote the kitaba.

Key Takeaway
A mukatab may validly write kitaba for his own slave, with wala' conditional on the timing of the slave's payment relative to the mukatab's freedom.

Iqrar (Confession) by the Mukatab
Rulings on confession by a mukatab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish valid from invalid confessions by a mukatab and apply the rules to scenarios involving debts, injuries, and property claims.

Core Idea
A mukatab's confession is valid only for debts and property in his own hands, not on behalf of others.

The mukatab is a slave with a contract to earn freedom. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the limits of his ability to make confessions (iqrar). While he can confess to debts and property for trade, he cannot confess on behalf of his child or others. Let's explore the rulings step by step.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the General Rule
The mukatab's confession regarding debt, property, and receipt is valid because he needs these for trade.
This is the baseline: iqrar by the mukatab is generally permissible for matters affecting his own liability.
2
Identify the Exception
He cannot confess against his child born during kitaba regarding a crime (jinaaya) because it is a confession against another person.
The child is independent, so the confession imposes liability on another, which is invalid.
3
Analyze Conditional Validity
If the child dies leaving property, the confession becomes relevant; the father becomes the opponent. Similarly, if the child earns property and the father takes it, the confession is effective on that property.
The confession remains invalid initially, but subsequent events can make it operative.
4
Apply to the Slave Woman Case
If a man claims a slave woman in the mukatab's possession is his umm walad or mukataba, and the mukatab confirms, the confession is valid and he must hand her over (and her child).
This is a confession about property belonging to another, which is valid because it is like a deposit (wadi'ah).
5
Synthesize the Principle
The mukatab's iqrar is valid only for matters directly concerning his own obligations or property in his possession, not for imposing obligations on third parties.
This principle guides all the specific rulings in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز إقرار المكاتب بالدين والعين والاستيفاء لأنه لا بد للتجار منه. ولو أقر المكاتب على ولده المولود في الكتابة بجناية لم يجز إقراره لأنه إقرار على غيره، فإن مات الولد وترك مالاً كان ذلك لأبيه وأخر إقراره وصار هو الخصم في الجناية لأنه ظهر المقر له في حقه بإقراره، وكذا لو أقر على ولده بدين لم يجز فإن اكتسب الولد مالاً وأخذه الأب نفذ إقراره عليه في المال مكاتب أو مأذون في يده أمة ادعى رجل أنها أم ولده أو مكاتبته فصدقه المكاتب أو المأذون فيه جاز ويدفعها إليه، وكذلك إن كان معها ولد دفعه إليه لأن إقراره بالوديعة اهـ.
And the confession of the mukatab regarding debt, property, and receipt is permissible because there is no alternative for merchants. If the mukatab confesses against his child born during the kitaba regarding a crime, his confession is not permissible because it is a confession against another. If the child dies and leaves property, that is for his father, and his confession is delayed, and he becomes the opponent in the crime because the confessee appears in his right by his confession. Similarly, if he confesses against his child regarding a debt, it is not permissible. If the child earns property and the father takes it, his confession is effective against him regarding the property. If a mukatab or authorized slave has a slave woman in his hand, and a man claims that she is his umm walad or his mukataba, and the mukatab or authorized slave confirms him, it is permissible, and he hands her over to him. Similarly, if she has a child with her, he hands it over to him, because his confession is as a deposit. End.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage explains the rulings on iqrar (confession or admission) by the mukatab. The mukatab's confession is valid for debts, property, and receipt because he needs these for trade. However, his confession against his child (born during kitaba) is not valid because it is a confession against another. If the child dies, the confession becomes relevant. If the child earns money and the father takes it, the confession is effective. Also, if a man claims that a slave woman in the mukatab's possession is his umm walad or mukataba, and the mukatab confirms, it is valid and he must hand her over along with her child. The passage thus details the limits of the mukatab's capacity to confess.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrār): A legal confession or admission of a right or debt against oneself.
الجناية (al-jināya): A crime or injury that incurs a financial penalty or retaliation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Is the confession of a mukatab valid if he confesses that his child owes a debt?
A) Yes, always.
B) No, because it is a confession against another.
C) Yes, if the child earns property.
D) No, because the mukatab cannot confess at all.

Key Takeaway
A mukatab's confession is valid for debts and property in his own hands but not for obligations of others, unless he takes property from that person.

Conflicting Acknowledgments Involving a Mukatab and a Person of Unknown Lineage
Analysis of conflicting acknowledgments of slavery and freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that the later iqrar abrogates the earlier when the later party had the opportunity to reject the earlier, and state the outcome in a scenario with conflicting acknowledgments of slavery.

Core Idea
Timing matters: a later acknowledgment abrogates an earlier one if it could have been rejected.

In Hanafi law, acknowledgments (iqrar) are binding admissions. But what happens when two conflicting acknowledgments occur? This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a key principle: the later acknowledgment takes precedence if the later party had the opportunity to reject the earlier but did not. Understanding this rule is crucial for cases where multiple parties claim different statuses of slavery or freedom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their acknowledgments
Determine who is making each acknowledgment and what they are acknowledging (e.g., slavery to someone).
In the passage, the mukatab and the majhul al-nasab each make acknowledgments.
2
Determine the chronological order
Establish which acknowledgment came first and which came later.
The passage states the mukatab's acknowledgment is earlier, and the majhul al-nasab's is later.
3
Assess the ability to reject
Check whether the later party had the ability to reject the earlier acknowledgment. If they did not object and instead made their own, they are deemed to have accepted the earlier.
Here, the later party (majhul al-nasab) could have rejected the earlier but did not.
4
Apply the principle of abrogation
The later iqrar abrogates the earlier when the later party had the capacity to reject the earlier. This is because the later statement overrides the earlier one.
The passage explicitly states 'الآخر ناسخ لأولهما' – the latter abrogates the first.
5
Derive the final legal status
Based on the valid (later) acknowledgment, determine the status of each party (slave or free).
In the example, both become slaves of the mukatabah (the woman who is a mukatab).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المكاتب / باب ما يجوز للمكاتب أن يفعله وما لا يجوز
للمكاتب فقد اجتمع إقرارها وإقرار المكاتب سابق على إقرار مجهول النسب وآخر إلا قرارين ناسبخ لأولهما لأن للآخر رد الأول ولم يوجد الرد للثاني فصح فصار الاعتبار لإقرار مجهول النسب لأنه آخرهما، وهذا كرجل مجهول النسب أقر بأنه مملوك لعبد رجل وأقر مولى العبد وهو مجهول النسب أنه مملوك لهذا المقر فهما جميعاً مملوكان لعبد مجهول النسب أقر بالرق للمكاتبة والمولى للمكاتبة وهو المكاتب أقر للمكاتب بالرق لمجهول النسب صارا مملوكين للمكاتبة ا هـ مختصراً .
Book of the Mukatab / Chapter on what the mukatab may do and what he may not. For the mukatab, there has been combined her acknowledgment and the mukatab's acknowledgment preceding the acknowledgment of the one of unknown lineage, and the last [is] two acknowledgments that abrogate the earlier of them because the latter has the opportunity to reject the first, but there was no rejection of the second, so it is valid, so consideration is given to the acknowledgment of the one of unknown lineage because it is the later of the two. This is like a man of unknown lineage who acknowledged that he is a slave of a man's slave, and the master of the slave, who is of unknown lineage, acknowledged that he is a slave to this acknowledging person, so both are slaves of the slave of unknown lineage. He acknowledged slavery to the mukatabah and the master to the mukatabah, and he is the mukatab who acknowledged slavery to the mukatab of unknown lineage, so they become slaves of the mukatabah. End, summarized.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the commentary explaining a complex case of conflicting acknowledgments (iqrar) involving a mukatab and a person of unknown lineage (majhul al-nasab). The author, al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, discusses a scenario where there are two acknowledgments from different parties. The principle applied is that the later acknowledgment abrogates the earlier one if the later party had the ability to reject the earlier but did not. Here, the second acknowledgment is given precedence. The example illustrates: a person of unknown lineage acknowledges he is a slave of a man's slave; the master of that slave (also of unknown lineage) acknowledges that he (the master) is a slave to the first acknowledge. The result is that both become slaves of the mukatabah. This demonstrates the Hanafi rule that in cases of conflicting iqrar, the later one prevails if it cannot be rejected.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A legal acknowledgment or confession, in this context about lineage and slavery status.
مجهول النسب (majhul al-nasab): A person of unknown lineage, considered free unless evidence shows otherwise, but here they acknowledge slavery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The earlier acknowledgment
B) The later acknowledgment
C) The acknowledgment of the mukatab
D) The acknowledgment of the master

Key Takeaway
In conflicting acknowledgments, the later iqrar abrogates the earlier if the later party could have rejected it.

Wala' of a Mukatab Who Frees Another Slave: Detailed Scenarios
Detailed analysis of wala' rules for mukatab manumittors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rules for wala' when a mukatab frees another slave in various scenarios (both pay together, first becomes incapable, first dies) and identify who gets the wala' in each case.

Core Idea
The wala' of a slave freed by a mukatab goes to the mukatab only if he becomes free before the freed slave; otherwise to the master.

This lesson delves into the intricate rulings of wala' (patronage rights) when a mukatab (a slave under a manumission contract) frees another slave. The Hanafi school, relying on al-Asl and later commentaries, provides detailed scenarios based on the timing of the mukatab's freedom, his ability to pay, and his death. Understanding these cases is essential for correctly assigning inheritance rights from freed slaves.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the baseline rule: wala' belongs to the one who manumits, but a mukatab is partially slave, so his wala' is attributed to his master unless he becomes free first.
The phrase 'wal-wala'u liman a'taqa' is the basis.
2
Analyze the 'both pay together' scenario
If both the mukatab and the freed slave pay their kitabah at the same time, the wala' goes to the master, not the mukatab, because the mukatab is still a slave at the moment of freeing.
From Sharh al-Tahawi: 'wa in addaya ma'an 'utiqa wa thabata wala'uhuma min al-mawla.'
3
Analyze the 'first becomes incapable' scenario
If the first mukatab (the one who freed) becomes unable to pay and returns to slavery, the second mukatab remains a mukatab for the master, and the wala' is for the master.
Analogous to the authorized slave scenario.
4
Analyze the death scenarios
When the first mukatab dies, the outcome depends on whether he left sufficient funds to pay his kitabah. If yes, the second pays his kitabah to the first's estate, and wala' goes to the first's heir (e.g., his son). If no, the first's kitabah is cancelled, he becomes a slave, and the second remains a mukatab for the master.
Two subcases: with sufficient funds (no cancellation) and without (cancellation).
5
Note the key principle
Wala' once established cannot be transferred; only the direct manumittor (or his heir) holds it. This is why the timing of the mukatab's freedom is critical.
From al-Muhit: 'wa wala'u al-'itaqati mata thabata 'ala ahadin la yahtamilu al-naqla ila ghayrihi ka al-nasab.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ولاؤهما من المولى. وفي الأصل: وإن عجز الأول ورد في الرق ولم يؤد الثاني مكاتبته بعد بقي الثاني مكاتباً على حاله، ونظيره العبد المأذون له إذا أذن لعبده في التجارة ثم حجر على الأول بقي الثاني يصير فظهر قول المؤلف لو قال أو عتقا معاً بأداء مكاتبتهما لكان أولى ليفيد أن الولاء له في الحالتين. وفي نوادر ابن سماعة عن محمد : إذا مات الأول وقد حل ما على الثاني وقد ترك وفاء إلا أنه دين على الناس ولم He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (And the wala' is for him if he pays after his emancipation) because wala' belongs to the one who manumits, and the freed person of the first mukatab, and he is qualified for wala' at the time of the second's emancipation, and his ownership was complete in him at that time, so it is established for him by necessity. In Sharh al-Tahawi: If they both pay together, they are freed and their wala' is from the master. In al-Asl: If the first becomes incapable and returns to slavery, and the second has not paid his kitabah afterwards, the second remains a mukatab as he was. Its analogy is the authorized slave if he authorized his slave in trade then placed a restriction on the first, the second becomes a slave in reality to the master; if he emancipates him, his emancipation is effective. If the first did not become incapable but died before payment and the second also did not pay his kitabah, then there are two aspects...

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling that the wala' (right of inheritance from a freed slave) belongs to the mukatab who frees a slave, provided he (the mukatab) pays his kitabah and gains freedom before the freed slave. The commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details multiple scenarios: if both pay together, wala' goes to the master; if the first mukatab becomes incapable (cajaza), the second remains a mukatab for the master; if the first dies leaving sufficient funds, the second pays to his estate; various cases of timing and heirs are discussed. The principle is that wala' is established for the one who directly manumits, but since the mukatab is partially slave, his wala' is attributed to his master unless he becomes free. The analysis includes references to Sharh al-Tahawi, al-Asl, and Nawadir Ibn Sam'ah.

Key Terms
ولاء (wala'): Patronage right that gives inheritance from a freed slave to the manumittor.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract (kitabah) to earn his freedom by paying installments.
عجز ('ajaza): Incapacity; when a mukatab fails to pay his installments and is returned to slavery.
فسخ (faskh): Cancellation; termination of a contract by operation of law or judicial decree.

Key Takeaway
The wala' of a slave freed by a mukatab goes to the mukatab only if he becomes free before the freed slave; otherwise, it belongs to his master, and the timing of events is crucial.

Guardian's Authority Over Minor's Slave: General vs. Specific Agents
Ruling that father and executor for minor's slave have same powers as mukatab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between guardians with general authority and those with specific trade authority regarding marrying the minor's female slave and performing kitabah.

Core Idea
A guardian with general authority is like a mukatab; a trade-specific agent is not.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the scope of a guardian's power over a minor's slave. The key analogy is to the mukatab (a slave under contract of manumission). The rule hinges on whether the guardian's authority is general (covering all property matters) or specific (limited to trade). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi rulings on property management for minors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the guardians with general authority
List the figures: father, grandfather, executor, judge, and his trustee. They have broad authority over the minor's property.
These are the 'general' guardians.
2
Understand the analogy to the mukatab
The mukatab owns what he earns but is not fully free. Similarly, general guardians own (meaning have the right to manage) the minor's property, including non-trade acts like marrying the slave.
The analogy is about capacity, not ownership.
3
Apply the principle to trade-specific agents
Agents like mudarib, sharik, and ma'dhun (authorized agent) have authority only within trade. They cannot perform non-trade acts like marrying the female slave or kitabah.
This is the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf allows trade-specific agents to marry the female slave if benefit exists. The preferred view (mufta bihi) rejects this.
The dispute hinges on whether such an act falls under 'trade'.
5
Consider the special case of the full partner (sharik al-muwafadah)
Some texts treat him like a mukatab (general authority), others like a trade agent. The commentator favors the trade-agent view, which is more consistent with fiqh.
This reflects varying interpretations within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والأب والوصي في رقيق الصغير كالمكاتب لأن الأب والوصي كالمكاتب فيملكان ما يملكه المكاتب، والأصل فيه أن كان تصرفه عاماً في التجارة وغيرها يملك تزویج كالمكاتب والأب والجد والوصي والقاضي وأمينه فكل من كان تصرفه خاصاً بالتجارة كالمضارب والشريك والمأذون فلا يملك تزويج الأمة لا الكتابة عند الإمام ومحمد. وقال الثاني : يملك تزويج الأمة لأن فيه منفعة على ما بينا وجوابه أنه ليس من باب التجارة فلا يملكه، وجعل في النهاية شريك المفاوضة كالمكاتب وجعله في الكافي كالمأذون له في التجارة ولكل وجه قال الشارح جعله كالمأذون أشبه بالفقه
He said: And the father and executor regarding the minor's slave are like the mukatab because the father and executor are like the mukatab in that they own what the mukatab owns. The principle is that if his authority is general in trade and other matters, he owns marrying like the mukatab, as the father, grandfather, executor, judge, and his trustee. So whoever's authority is specific to trade, like the mudarib, partner, and authorized agent, they do not own marrying the female slave nor kitabah according to Imam and Muhammad. And the Second said: He owns marrying the female slave because there is benefit in it as we explained. The answer is that it is not of the category of trade, so he does not own it. In al-Nihayah, the partner of muwafadah is made like the mukatab, and in al-Kafi he is made like the authorized agent for trade, and each has a face. The commentator said: Making him like the authorized agent is more resembling fiqh.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage discusses the extent of authority of a guardian (father, executor, judge) over a minor's slave. It states that these guardians, who have general authority over the minor's property (including trade and non-trade matters), are like the mukatab in that they can perform acts that the mukatab can, such as marrying the slave? Actually, the text says they 'own marrying like the mukatab' meaning they can marry the slave? But earlier it said the mukatab cannot marry his male slave. The context is about 'tazwij al-ama' (marrying the female slave). The guardians with general authority can marry the female slave because it brings benefit. Those with specific trade authority (mudarib, partner, authorized agent) cannot. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say they cannot marry the female slave or perform kitabah; Abu Yusuf says they can if there is benefit. The preferred view is that they cannot because it is not trade. The passage also mentions a variant ruling regarding the partner of muwafadah (full partnership).

Key Terms
رقيق الصغير (raqīq al-ṣaghīr): The slave of a minor child.
وصي (waṣī): Executor of a will, appointed to manage a minor's affairs.
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has entered a contract of manumission (kitabah) with his master, agreeing to pay a sum for freedom.
تصرف عام (taṣarruf ʿāmm): General authority encompassing both trade and non-trade matters.

Key Takeaway
A guardian with general authority over a minor's property is like a mukatab and can perform non-trade acts; trade-specific agents cannot.

Inclusion of Blood Relatives in a Mukatab's Kitabah
Ruling with proof: inclusion of blood relatives in kitabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a mukatab's purchase of a blood relative includes that relative as a subsidiary in his kitabah and explain the legal implications of subsidiary status.

Core Idea
A mukatab's blood relative purchased becomes a subsidiary in his kitabah, not a separate mukatab.

When a mukatab (a slave under a kitabah contract) purchases his father, son, or any blood relative, that relative does not become free or a separate mukatab. Instead, he is included as a subsidiary (taba') in the original mukatab's kitabah, working to pay off the same kitabah amount. This ruling is based on facilitating filial piety (silat al-rahm) and the principle that the subsidiary follows the primary. The passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq details this ruling and distinguishes between relatives born during the kitabah and those purchased later.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Ruling
Determine what action triggers the ruling: a mukatab purchases a blood relative (father, son, etc.). The ruling is that the relative becomes included in the mukatab's kitabah as a subsidiary.
The relative does not become a separate mukatab but is attached to the existing contract.
2
Understand the Reasoning (Ta'lil)
The ruling is based on facilitating silat al-rahm: since the mukatab cannot emancipate the relative directly (he does not own the 'neck' fully), the relative is made a mukatab by inclusion. This is a concession to allow the relative to work toward freedom.
Compare to a free person who would be emancipated immediately.
3
Distinguish Between Born and Purchased Subsidiaries
A child born to the mukatab during the kitabah is fully subsidiary: his status continues even after the mukatab's death, and he must work for the installments. A purchased relative is subsidiary only during the mukatab's life; if the mukatab dies, the purchased relative is sold.
The born child's connection is by both ownership and partiality (juz'iyya) in reality; the purchased relative's connection is only juristically.
4
Apply the Order of Precedence
Among subsidiarily included relatives, the son takes precedence over the father, and a born child takes precedence over a purchased one. This order affects who is prioritized if the kitabah cannot support all.
The father is mentioned first in the text for glorification, but in legal order the son comes first.
5
Note the Limitations
The father cannot be sold during the son's life. If the original mukatab dies, the subsidiary father is sold (unlike a born child). The relative does not become a principal mukatab.
The phrase 'tukatib 'alayhi' (is made mukatab upon him) indicates attachment, not independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( ولو اشترى أباه أو ابنه تكاتب عليه لما ذكر ما هو داخل في الكتابة بطريق الأصالة وأنهاه شرع يذكر ما هو داخل بطريق التبع والتبع يتلو الأصل، وإنما يكاتب عليه لأن المكاتب يملك الكتابة وإن لم يملك العتق فيجعل مكاتباً . تخفيفاً للصلة بقدر الإمكان لأنه لما تعذر الإعتاق صار مكاتباً مثله للتعذر بخلاف الحر فإنه يملك الرقبة ولا تعذر في حقه فيعتق عليه كما تقدم في بابه بيانه وذكر الابن والأب وقع اتفاقاً لأن هذا الحكم لا يختص بهما بل جميع من له قرابة الولادة يدخلون في كتابه تبعاً له. وأقواهم دخولاً المولود في الكتابة يكون حكمه حكم أبيه حتى إذا مات أبوه ولم يترك شيئاً يسعى على نجوم أبيه، والولد المشتري يؤدي البدل حالاً وإلا يرد في الرق، وإنما كان كذلك لأن المولود في الكتابة تبعيته ثابتة بالملك والبعضية الثابتة حقيقة وقت العقد بخلاف المشتري فإن تبعيته ثابتة بالملك والبغضية فيهما حكماً في حق العقد لا حقيقة في حقه لأنه لا بعضية بينهما حقيقة بعد الانفصال. قال الأكمل : وتقديم الأب في الذكر للتعظيم . أما في الترتيب فيقدم الابن على الأب سواء كان مولوداً أو مشتري في الكتابة والمولود مقدم على المشتري فالمولود يظهر حاله في الحياة وبعد الممات كما تقدم، والمشتري في حال الحياة فقط كما تقدم، والأب يحرم بيعه حال حياة ولده ولم يقبل منه البدل بعد موته حالاً ولا مؤجلاً ! . وإنما قال «تكاتب عليه» ولم يقل صار مكاتباً لأنه لو صار مكاتباً لصار أصلاً ولبقيت الكتابة بعد موت المكاتب الأصلي وليس كذلك، بل إذا مات المكاتب يباع الأب
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if he buys his father or his son, they become included in his kitabah) because of what was mentioned. That which is included in the kitabah in a primary way, and he ended by mentioning what is included in a subsidiary way, and the subsidiary follows the primary. He is made a mukatab because the mukatab owns the kitabah even if he does not own emancipation, so he is made a mukatab as a facilitation for filial piety as much as possible. Since emancipation is impossible, he becomes a mukatab like him due to impossibility, unlike a free person, for he owns the neck and there is no impossibility regarding him, so he is emancipated through him as previously explained in its chapter. Mentioning the son and father happened incidentally because this ruling is not specific to them but all who have a blood relationship enter into his kitabah as subordinates to him. The strongest in entry is the child born during kitabah; his ruling is the same as his father's so that if his father dies without leaving anything, he works for his father's installments. The purchased child pays the replacement immediately, otherwise he is returned to slavery. This is because the child born during kitabah has his subsidiary status established by ownership and partiality established in reality at the time of contract, unlike the purchaser, for his subsidiary status is established by ownership and partiality in them juristically regarding the contract, not in reality regarding him because there is no partiality between them in reality after separation. Al-Akmal said: Preferring the father in mention is for glorification. As for order, the son takes precedence over the father, whether born or purchased in kitabah, and the born one takes precedence over the purchased one. The born one's status appears in life and after death as previously, the purchased one only in life as previously. The father is prohibited from sale during the life of his son, and the replacement is not accepted from him after his death immediately nor deferred. He only said 'they become included in his kitabah' and did not say 'he becomes a mukatab' because if he became a mukatab, he would become a principal and the kitabah would remain after the death of the original mukatab, but it is not so; rather, if the mukatab dies, the father is sold.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling that if a mukatab purchases his father or son (or any blood relative through birth), that relative becomes included in his kitabah contract. This is a subsidiary inclusion (taba'iyyah) as a form of support (silat al-rahm). The mukatab cannot emancipate them directly because he lacks full ownership, so they are included as mukatabs alongside him. The text distinguishes between a child born during the kitabah (who is fully subsidiary even after death) and a purchased child (subsidiary only during the mukatab's life). The ruling extends to all blood relatives, but the order of precedence is specified: the son comes before the father, and the born child before the purchased one. The father cannot be sold during the son's life, and if the original mukatab dies, the father is sold (his subsidiary status does not survive death).

Key Terms
قرابة الولادة (qarābat al-wilādah): Blood relationship through birth, including ascendants and descendants.
تبع (taba'): Subsidiary or dependent; in this context, a relative who is included in the kitabah as a follower of the original mukatab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The father becomes free immediately
B) The father is included in the mukatab's kitabah as a subsidiary
C) The father must be sold
D) The mukatab's kitabah is void

Key Takeaway
A mukatab's purchase of a blood relative includes that relative as a subsidiary in his kitabah, facilitating filial piety while preserving the contractual structure.

Buying Brother or Mahram: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn on Kitabah Inclusion
Intra-Hanafi dispute on brother inclusion in kitabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether a mukatab's purchase of a mahram relative includes that relative in his kitabah, and evaluate the reasoning based on qiyas and istihsan.

Core Idea
A mukatab's limited ownership restricts sila to direct lineage; mahram relatives are included only if they are parents or children.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The question is whether a mukatab (a slave who has contracted kitabah to earn his freedom) automatically includes his brother or other mahram relatives in the kitabah when he purchases them. Abu Hanifa says no; the Sahibayn say yes. The dispute turns on the scope of silat al-rahm (filial piety) and the nature of the mukatab's ownership. We will examine the proofs on both sides and the Hanafi method of reconciling conflicting analogies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue and positions
Read the opening statement. The passage deals with whether a mukatab's purchase of his brother or other mahram relative makes that relative a part of the kitabah contract. State the views of Abu Hanifa (not included) and the Sahibayn (included).
The phrase 'لا يكاتب عليه' means he is not made a mukatab for that relative.
2
Understand the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn argue that the obligation of silat al-rahm extends to all mahram relatives, as evidenced by six analogous rulings: automatic emancipation, obligatory maintenance, no recourse, no hand-cutting for theft, etc. If these apply to free persons, the mukatab should also fulfill sila by including such relatives in kitabah.
Their proof is qiyas from a free person's obligations to a mukatab's capacity.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's counter-argument
Abu Hanifa distinguishes the mukatab's incomplete ownership from full ownership. The mukatab has earnings but not real ownership due to slavery. Thus his earnings suffice only for direct lineage (parents and children), as maintenance of a sibling is not obligatory unless wealthy. Including other mahram would overburden him.
He also points out that mahram relatives share features with paternal cousins, who are not included in kitabah.
4
Trace the qiyas method: dual analogy
Abu Hanifa notes that mahram relatives resemble birth relatives in some rulings (marriage prohibition, maintenance) and paternal cousins in others (qisas, testimony). He gives each similarity its due: emancipation follows birth relations, kitabah follows paternal cousins. This balances the analogies.
This is a sophisticated use of qiyas with multiple shabah (resemblances).
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative view)
The text indicates that the relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's view, supported by istihsan over strict qiyas. Later Hanafi scholars, including the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq, favor this position.
The passage also cites al-Dhakhirah: uncles and aunts are excluded by istihsan, though qiyas would include them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولو أخاه ونحوه لا) يعني لو اشترى أخاه أو غيره من محارمه لا يكاتب عليه عند الإمام. وقالا : يكاتب عليه لأن وجوب الصلة تشمل القرابة المحرمة للنكاح ولهذا يعتق على الحل كل ذي رحم محرم منه وتجب نفقتهم عليه ولا يرجع فيما وبه لهم ولا يقطع يده إذا سرق منهم إلى غير ذلك الأحكام فكذا هذا الحكم. وللإمام أن للمكاتب كسباً وليس له ملك حقيقة لوجود ما ينافيه وهو الرق، ولهذا لو اشترى أمة ولده لا يفسد نكاحه ويجوز دفع الزكاة إليه ولو وجد كنزاً والكسب يكفي للصلة في الأولاد ألا ترى أن القادر على الكسب يخاطب بنفقة الولد والوالد ولا يكفي في غيرها حتى لا يخاطب الأخ بنفقة أخيه إلا إذا كان موسراً والدخول في الكتابة بطريق الصلة فتختص بقرابة الولاد، ولأن هذه قرابة تشبه بني الأعمام في حق بعض الأحكام كحل الحلية وجريان القصاص من الجانبين وقبول الشهادة ودفع الزكاة إليه، وتشبه الولاد في حق حرمة المناكحة ووجوب النفقة وحرمة الجمع بين اثنين منهن فألحقناها بالولاد في العتق وبني الأعمام في الكتابة توفيراً على الشبهين حظهما والعمل على هذا الوجه أولى من العمل على العكس. وفي الذخيرة لو اشترى العم والعمة فالقياس أن يصيرا مثله في الكتابة وفي الاستحسان لا يكاتب عليهما ا هـ .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if he buys his brother or similar, no) meaning if he buys his brother or other mahram relatives, he is not made a mukatab according to Imam (Abu Hanifa). And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He is made a mukatab because the obligation of filial piety includes relatives prohibited for marriage, and therefore, [a free person] emancipates any such relative from him, and their maintenance is obligatory upon him, and he does not reclaim what he gave them, and his hand is not cut off if he steals from them, and other rulings. So likewise this ruling. And for the Imam, the mukatab has earnings but does not have real ownership because of what contradicts it, which is slavery. Hence if he buys a female slave of his son, his marriage is not corrupted, and zakat can be given to him, and if he finds treasure, and earning suffices for filial piety towards children. Do you not see that one capable of earning is addressed with maintenance of child and parent, but it does not suffice for others, so a brother is not addressed with maintenance of his brother unless he is wealthy. And entering into kitabah by way of filial piety is specific to birth relatives. And because this kinship resembles that of paternal cousins in some rulings, such as permissibility of adornment, execution of qisas from both sides, acceptance of testimony, and giving zakat to him. And it resembles birth relatives in prohibition of marriage, obligation of maintenance, and prohibition of combining two such women. So we attached it to birth relatives in emancipation and to paternal cousins in kitabah to give each similarity its due, and acting on this is preferable to the opposite. In al-Dhakhirah: If he buys an uncle or aunt, by analogy they would become like him in kitabah, but by istihsan they are not made mukatab for them.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. Abu Hanifa (the Imam) holds that if a mukatab buys his brother or other mahram relatives (those prohibited in marriage due to blood relation), they are NOT included in his kitabah. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) argue that they should be included because the duty of filial piety (silat al-rahm) extends to all mahram relatives, as evidenced by various rulings like emancipation, maintenance, non-recourse, and non-cutting of hand for theft. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the mukatab's ownership is incomplete due to slavery, so his earnings are only sufficient for filial piety towards direct ascendants and descendants (wilad). Other relatives are not owed maintenance unless wealthy, so they should not be included in kitabah. Furthermore, mahram relatives (like siblings) share characteristics with both paternal cousins (in matters like qisas, testimony) and with birth relatives (in marriage prohibition, maintenance). Abu Hanifa gives each similarity its due: emancipation (which requires strong connection) is only for birth relatives, while kitabah (a weaker connection) is for paternal cousins, not for siblings. The text also notes that by strict analogy (qiyas), uncles and aunts would be included, but by istihsan (juristic preference), they are not. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa, i.e., no inclusion.

Key Terms
محارم (maḥārim): Relatives who are permanently prohibited in marriage due to blood relationship.
صلة (ṣilat al-raḥm): Filial piety or maintaining family ties, which includes financial support.
الكتابة (al-kitābah): A contract between a master and slave whereby the slave earns money to buy his freedom.
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Juristic preference, allowing departure from strict analogy for a stronger reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Does Abu Hanifa include a brother in the mukatab's kitabah?
A) Yes, because he is a mahram
B) No, because sibling maintenance is not obligatory unless wealthy
C) Yes, if the brother agrees
D) No, because the brother is not a descendant

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school resolves this dispute by limiting the mukatab's obligation of sila to direct ancestry, drawing a distinction between the mukatab's incomplete ownership and a free person's full ownership.

Sale of Umm Walad Purchased with Her Child by a Mukatab
Ruling on sale of umm walad with child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling an umm walad when purchased with her child by a mukatab, and distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions when purchased without the child.

Core Idea
An umm walad purchased with her child cannot be sold – she follows the child's immunity.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in Hanafi fiqh concerning a mukatab (slave under a contract of manumission) who purchases an umm walad (a female slave who has borne a child to her master) along with her child. The central question is whether the mukatab can later sell this umm walad. The passage provides the ruling and its rationale, along with an important intra-Hanafi disagreement when the umm walad is purchased without her child. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the concept of subordination (tab'iyya) and the special status of the umm walad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what action is being ruled upon. Here, the act is the sale of an umm walad by a mukatab who purchased her together with her child. The ruling is that this sale is not permissible (la yajuz).
The ruling is immediate: prohibition of sale.
2
Understand the rationale (talil)
The reason given is that the child, having entered into the kitabah (contract of manumission), cannot be sold. Since the mother is a subordinate (taba') of the child, her sale is also prohibited to avoid separating them.
The child's immunity extends to the mother via subordination.
3
Note the condition stated by 'with him'
The text qualifies the ruling with 'with him' (ma'ahu), meaning the prohibition only applies when the umm walad is purchased together with her child. If she is purchased alone, the ruling differs.
This sets up the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
According to Abu Hanifa, if the umm walad is bought without the child, the mukatab may sell her. However, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that he may not sell her even in that case.
The text begins to mention their position but is cut off; we know from other sources that the Sahibayn disagree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولو اشترى أم ولده معه لم يجز بيعها) يعني لو اشترى زوجته منها لم يجز له بيعها لأن الولد لما دخل في الكتابة امتنع بيعه لما ذكرنا فتتبعه أمه فامتنع بيعها لأنها تبع له ولا تدخل في كتابته حتى لا يعتق بعتقه ولا ينفسخ النكاح لأنه لم يملكها، وكذا المكاتبة إذا اشترت زوجها غير أن لها أن يتبعه كيفما كان لأن الحرية لم تثبت من جهتها على ما بينا. قيد بقوله «معه لأنه لو ملكها بدون الولد جاز له بيعها عند الإمام. وقالا : ليس له
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if he buys an umm walad along with him, it is not permissible to sell her) meaning if he buys his wife from her, it is not permissible for him to sell her because the child, having entered into kitabah, its sale is prevented as we mentioned, so its mother follows it, so her sale is prevented because she is a subordinate to it, and she does not enter into his kitabah so that she is not freed by his emancipation nor is the marriage dissolved because he did not own her. Similarly, the female mukatab if she buys her husband, except that she may follow him in whatever way because freedom is not established from her side as we explained. He qualified it by 'with him' because if he owned her without the child, it would be permissible for him to sell her according to Imam. And the two said: He does not have [the right].
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage rules that if a mukatab buys an umm walad (a female slave who has borne a child to her master) along with her child, he cannot sell her. The reason is that the child becomes included in the kitabah (as per the previous ruling), and the mother, being a subordinate (taba') of the child, also becomes unsellable. However, she does not enter the kitabah contract itself; she remains a slave of the mukatab but cannot be sold because that would separate her from her child. If the mukatab buys the umm walad without the child, then according to Abu Hanifa, he can sell her (since there is no child to follow). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree and say he cannot sell her either (the text cuts off but implies their position). The passage also notes a parallel with a female mukatab buying her husband: she may follow him, but the rulings differ slightly because her husband's freedom does not come from her.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, giving her special status; she becomes free upon her master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
An umm walad purchased with her child cannot be sold due to subordination; without the child, there is an intra-Hanafi disagreement on permissibility.

Children of a Mukatab's Slave Woman: Kitaba and Earnings
Explaining that a child born to the mukatab's slave woman enters the kitaba and its earnings belong to the child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the status of a child born to a mukatab's slave woman and explain the effect of the mukatab's claim of paternity on the child's inclusion in the kitaba.

Core Idea
A child born to a mukatab's slave woman enters the kitaba and his earnings belong to him if the mukatab claims paternity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies what happens when a mukatab (a slave with a contract of manumission) fathers a child with his slave woman. The ruling may seem counterintuitive because a mukatab does not have the right of concubinage, yet the child is still attributed to him. We will explore the legal reasoning and the practical consequences for the child's status and earnings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The passage states: if a child is born to a mukatab from his slave woman, the child enters the kitaba (the contract) and the child's earnings belong to the child.
The child becomes a co-party in the kitaba, and its earnings are separate, not for the mukatab.
2
Understand the Legal Reasoning (Ta'lil)
The reasoning: by the mukatab's claim of paternity (da'wa al-nasab), the child's lineage is established. Consequently, the child follows the father in the kitaba. The child is considered the mukatab's property (slave) in a limited sense, so earnings generated by the child are held for the child's own benefit (as a trust for eventual freedom).
The child's earnings are not for the mukatab; they accumulate for the child's eventual manumission.
3
Address the Objection about Concubinage
An objection is raised: the mukatab does not have the right of concubinage (tamalluk al-sirriyah), so how can he have a child from a slave woman? The answer distinguishes between ownership for intercourse (which is prohibited) and the effect of an actual claim. The prohibition does not prevent paternity from being established if he claims it.
The mukatab's lack of permission does not negate the legal effect of his claim.
4
Consider the Joint Ownership Example
The passage cites al-Mabsut: if a slave woman is jointly owned between a free man and a mukatab, and the mukatab claims the child, he must pay half the 'aqr (damages for intercourse) and half the value of the slave woman, but nothing for the child (since the mukatab is like a free man in not being liable for the child's value).
This example illustrates the principle in a more complex scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله تعالى: (وإن ولد له ولد من أمته تكاتب عليه وكسبه له) لأنه بالدعوى ثبت النسب له فيتبعه في الكتابة وكان كسب الولد له لأنه في حكم مملوكه فكان كسبه له، وكذا لو ولدت المكاتبة ولداً دخل في كتابتها كما سنذكره. قال في العناية: واعترض عليه بأن المكاتب لا يملك التسري فمن أين له ولد من الأمة حتى يدخل في الكتابة؟ وأجيب بأن معنى قولنا لا يملك لا يحل له وطء أمة لكن إن وطئ وادعى النسب ثبت. قال في المبسوط: جارية بين حر ومكاتب ولدت ولداً فادعاه المكاتب قال: الولد ولده والجارية أم ولده ويضمن نصف عقرها ونصف قيمتها، ولا يضمن من قيمة الولد شيئاً لأن المكاتب كالحر ولا يضمن. ولو ولدت
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if a child is born to him from his slave woman, he is included in the kitaba and his earnings are for him) because by the claim (of paternity) the lineage is established for him, so he follows him in the kitaba, and the earnings of the child are for him because he is in the ruling of his slave, so his earnings are for him. Similarly, if a female mukatab gives birth to a child, the child enters her kitaba, as we will mention. He said in al-Inayah: An objection was made that the mukatab does not have the right of concubinage, so how can he have a child from a slave woman to enter the kitaba? The answer is that the meaning of our statement 'he does not own (tamalluk) concubinage' means that it is not permissible for him to have intercourse with a slave woman, but if he does and claims paternity, it is established. He said in al-Mabsut: A slave woman owned jointly between a free man and a mukatab gave birth to a child, and the mukatab claimed it; he said: The child is his, and the slave woman is his umm walad, and he guarantees half of her 'aqr (damages for intercourse) and half of her value, but he does not guarantee anything from the value of the child because the mukatab is like a free man and does not guarantee.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that a child born to a mukatab's slave woman enters into the mukatab's kitaba (the contract of manumission) and the child's earnings belong to him (the child). The reasoning: by the mukatab's claim of paternity, the child's lineage is established, so the child follows him in the kitaba. The child is considered the mukatab's slave, so his earnings are for the child (as a trust for his own freedom). The same applies if a female mukatab gives birth: the child enters her kitaba. An objection is raised: a mukatab does not have the right of concubinage (tamalluk al-sirriyah), so how can he have a child from a slave woman? The answer: 'does not own' means it is not permissible for him, but if he does it and claims paternity, the lineage is established. An example from al-Mabsut: a slave woman jointly owned between a free man and a mukatab gives birth; the mukatab claims the child. He must pay half the 'aqr (compensation for intercourse with a slave woman) and half her value, but nothing for the child because the mukatab is like a free man in this respect (no guarantee for the child). The text ends abruptly; likely continues with further details.

Key Terms
أمة (amah): A female slave.
دعوى النسب (da'wa al-nasab): Claim of paternity; the mukatab must claim the child for it to be established as his.
كتابة (kitaba): A contract of manumission between a master and a slave, where the slave agrees to pay a sum for freedom.
عقر ('aqr): Compensation paid for intercourse with a slave woman, equivalent to the loss in value due to defloration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the child born to a mukatab's slave woman when the mukatab claims paternity?
A) The child is free immediately.
B) The child enters the mukatab's kitaba and his earnings are for the mukatab.
C) The child enters the mukatab's kitaba and his earnings are for the child.
D) The child remains a slave of the master and is not affected by the kitaba.

Key Takeaway
The child born to a mukatab's slave woman enters the kitaba upon the mukatab's claim of paternity, and the child's earnings are reserved for his own eventual freedom.
