Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Manumission of a Mukatab in Marad al-Mawt
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kitaba manumission in terminal illness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on the amount a mukatab must strive for when manumitted in marad al-mawt, and explain the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) in the outcome for the slave.

Core Idea
In manumitting a mukatab in terminal illness, Imam Abu Hanifa grants the slave an option; the Sahibayn fix the amount to a fraction of value.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the manumission of a mukatab (a slave under a kitaba contract) by a master in his terminal illness (marad al-mawt). The central issue is what amount the mukatab must strive to earn after being freed: is it a fraction of his market value or a fraction of the remaining kitaba debt? The passage presents the differing views of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, along with several subsidiary rulings from the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The act is the master manumitting his mukatab slave while the master is in marad al-mawt. The master has not received any payment of the kitaba debt before death.
The manumission is treated as a bequest, subject to the one-third rule.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the mukatab has a choice: he may strive for two-thirds of his market value or two-thirds of the remaining kitaba debt, whichever is less burdensome.
The text says: 'وقال الإمام: يسعى في ثلثي قيمته وإن شاء سعى في ثلثي ما عليه من الكتابة'.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the mukatab must strive for two-thirds of his market value only, and the kitaba contract is void. In another scenario, they say one-third of value.
The text: 'وعندهما يسعى في ثلث قيمته' in one variant.
4
Analyze the underlying reasoning
Abu Hanifa views the kitaba as a contract that can be partially fulfilled; the Sahibayn see the manumission as a charitable act like a bequest, thus tied to the slave's value.
No explicit dalil in text, but reasoning is based on the nature of the transaction.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa, the slave can choose a lower amount; under the Sahibayn, the amount is fixed, which may be higher. This affects the slave's ability to gain freedom.
Thamarah al-khilaf: the slave's financial burden differs.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيدنا وقيمته ألف لأنه لو كان بالعكس ففي العتابية : وإن كاتبه على ألف إلى سنة وقيمته ألفان ولم تجز الورثة أدى ثلث القيمة حالاً أورد رقيقاً في قولهم جميعاً لأن المحاباة في القدر وهو إسقاط ألف درهم والتأخير وهو تأجيله الألف فلم يصح تصرفه في ثلثي القيمة لا في الإسقاط ولا في حق التأخير اهـ. وفي المحيط مريض كاتب عبده على قدر قيمته فمات ولا مال غيره يقال عجل لي ثلثي البدل والثلث مؤجلاً كما هو، فإن لم يفعل يرد في الرق. وفيه أيضاً : لو كاتب عبده في الصحة ثم أقر في مرضه باستيفاء بدلها فصدق لأن حق الورثة لم يكن متعلقاً بالعقد فصح إقراره بالاستيفاء كما لو باع أجنبياً في الصحة في ثم أقر باستيفاء الثمن في المرض، ولو كان عليه دين محيط لم يقبل في شيء ويعتق العبد يزعمه ويؤخذ بالكتابة، ولو قال إن مت فكاتبوا هذا العبد تصح الوصية لأنه يملك عتقه فيملك الإيصاء، ومن كاتب عبده في مرضه ولا مال له غيره فأجازه الورثة في حياتهم فلهم الإبقاء بعد موته. ولو كاتب عبده في صحته على ألف وقيمته خمسمائة فأعتقه عند الموت ولم يقبض شيئاً حتى مات سعى في ثلثي قيمته عندهما وتبطل الكتابة. وقال الإمام: يسعى في ثلثی قیمته وإن شاء سعى في ثلثي ما عليه من الكتابة فإن قبض المولى خمسمائة ثم أعتقه في مرضه، فإن كان المقبوض هالكاً لم يحسب له شيء مما أدى وصار مال الكتابة ما بقي فيسعى في ثلثي كتابته لأن ثلثي كتابته وثلثي ما بقي من كتابته سواء. وعندهما يسعى في ثلث قيمته، ولو أدى المكاتب المائة أعتقه في مرضه سعى في ثلثي المائة بالإجماع اهـ. وفي شرح الطحاوي : من أعتق مكاتبه وهو مريض ينظر؛ إن كان يخرج من الثلث عتق مجاناً، وإن كان الثلث ولم تجز الورثة ينظر إلى ثلثي قيمته وإلى ثلثي بدل الكتابة وله الخيار يسعى في أيهما شاء عند الإمام وظاهر قوله عبده أن ملكه كامل له وإنما باشر العقد بنفسه ليحترز عما إذا كان بين صحيح ومريض. قال في المحيط : وإن كان العبد بين رجلين مرض أحدهما وكاتبه الصحيح بإذنه جاز وليس للوارث إبطاله، وكذا إذا أذن له في القبض وقبض بدل الكتابة ثم مات المريض لم يكن للوارث أن يأخذ منه شيئاً. وفي الجامع : مكاتب أقر لمولاه في صحته بألف درهم وقد كان المولى كاتبه على ألف وأقر المكاتب في صحته لأجنبي بألف درهم فمرض المكاتب وفي يده ألف فقضاها المولى من المكاتب فمات من ذلك المرض وليس له مال غيرها فالألف تقسم بين المولى والأجنبي على ثلاثة أسهم : سهمان للمولى وسهم للأجنبي. ولو أن المكاتب أدى الألف إلى المولى من الدين الذي أقر به ثم مات فالأجنبي أحق بهذه الألف وبطل دين المولى ومكاتبته، وإن مات عن غير وفاء فرد في الرق ومات على ملك المولى ويبطل دين المولى وكتابته ولو لم يقبض المولى الألف ومات وتركها فهي للأجنبي. ولو ترك المكاتب ابناً ولد له في الكتابة فالأجنبي أحق بهذه الألف أيضاً المولى ابن المكاتب ويبيع بالدين والمكتابة وإذا أدى الابن الكتابة والدين لا ينقض القضاء للأجنبي. ولو أن رجلاً كاتب عبده على ألف درهم في صحته وأقرضه أجنبي ألف درهم ثم مرض المكاتب وأقرضه درهم بمعاينة الشهود فسرقت من المكاتب وفي يد المكاتب ألف درهم أخرى فقضاها المولى فالمولى أحق بها من الأجنبي بخلاف ما لو اشترى المكاتب في مرضه عبداً . المولى بألف درهم ولرجل أجنبي على المكاتب ألف فهلك العبد وفي يد المكاتب ألف درهم غير فقضاها المولى من ثمن العبد فمات المكاتب من مرضه ذلك ولم يترك وفاء، فما قبض المولى من ثمن العبد لا يسلم للمولى وإن كان البيع وقبض الثمن بمعاينة الشهود فيسترد الألف ويدفع إلى الأجنبي. والفرق أن صورة القرض المماثلة ظاهرة فيقدم المولى ولم تظهر في صورة البيع فقدم الأجنبي فتأمل وفيه أيضاً : كاتب عبده على ألفين وله ابنان حران وهما وارثاه فمرض المكاتب وأقر لأحد الابنين بألف درهم وأقر للمولى بدين ألف درهم فمات
We restricted [the case] to his value being one thousand because if it were the opposite, then in al-Utabiyya: If he wrote kitaba for one thousand to a year and his value is two thousand, and the heirs do not permit, he pays one-third of the value (i.e., of the price?) immediately or returns to slavery, in all their sayings, because the concession is in the amount—dropping one thousand dirhams—and the deferment—postponing the thousand; thus his disposition is not valid in two-thirds of the value, neither in dropping nor in deferment. And in al-Muhit: A sick person writes kitaba for his slave for the amount of his value, then dies with no other property; he is told: expedite two-thirds of the consideration for me, and one-third deferred as it is; if he does not, he returns to slavery. And in it also: If he writes kitaba in health then acknowledges in his illness the receipt of its consideration, it is accepted, because the right of the heirs was not attached to the contract; thus his acknowledgment of receipt is valid, as if he sold to a third party in health then acknowledged receipt of the price in illness. And if he is in overwhelming debt, he is not accepted in anything, and the slave becomes free by his claim and is taken for the kitaba. And if he said: 'If I die, then write kitaba for this slave,' the will is valid because he owns his manumission so he owns the bequest. And whoever writes kitaba for his slave in his illness with no other property and the heirs permit it during their lifetime, then they have the right to keep it after his death. And if he writes kitaba for his slave in health for one thousand while his value is five hundred, then he manumits him at death without having received anything, and then dies, the slave strives for two-thirds of his value according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?) and the kitaba is void. Imam Abu Hanifa said: he strives for two-thirds of his value, or if he wishes, he strives for two-thirds of what he owes from the kitaba. If the master received five hundred then manumits him in his illness: if what was received is lost, nothing is credited from what he paid, and the kitaba debt remains as what is left; he strives for two-thirds of his kitaba because two-thirds of his kitaba and two-thirds of what remains of his kitaba are the same. According to them, he strives for one-third of his value. And if the mukatab paid one hundred and he manumits him in his illness, he strives for two-thirds of the one hundred by consensus. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: Whoever manumits his mukatab while sick: if it comes out of one-third, he is free gratis; if it is from one-third and the heirs do not permit, he looks to two-thirds of his value and two-thirds of the kitaba consideration: he has the choice to strive for whichever he wishes according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The apparent meaning of his word 'his slave' is that his ownership is complete; he only enters the contract himself to exclude the case where the slave is between a healthy and a sick person. Al-Muhit says: If the slave is between two men, one falls ill and the healthy one writes kitaba with his permission, it is valid and the heir cannot annul it; likewise if he permits him to receive and he receives the kitaba consideration, then the sick one dies, the heir cannot take anything from him. And in al-Jami': A mukatab acknowledged to his master in health one thousand dirhams while the master had written kitaba for one thousand, and the mukatab acknowledged to a third party one thousand dirhams; then the mukatab fell ill, having one thousand dirhams in hand, and the master claimed it from the mukatab; then the mukatab died from that illness with no other property; the one thousand is divided between the master and the third party into three shares: two shares for the master, one for the third party. And if the mukatab paid the one thousand to the master from the debt he acknowledged, then died, the third party is more entitled to this thousand, and the master's debt and kitaba are void. And if he died without payment, he is returned to slavery and dies as the master's property; the master's debt and kitaba are void. And if the master did not receive the thousand and died, leaving it, it is for the third party. And if the mukatab left a son born during the kitaba, then the third party is also more entitled to this thousand—the master [takes] the son of the mukatab, and he sells for the debt and the kitaba; and if the son pays the kitaba and the debt, it does not nullify the judgment for the third party. And if a man wrote kitaba for his slave for one thousand dirhams in health, and a third party lent the slave one thousand dirhams, then the mukatab fell ill and another lent him a dirham in the presence of witnesses, and it was stolen from the mukatab, and the mukatab had one thousand dirhams in hand; the master claimed it, so the master is more entitled to it than the third party, unlike if the mukatab bought a slave from the master for one thousand dirhams in his illness, and a third party has a claim of one thousand on the mukatab; the slave perished, and the mukatab had one thousand dirhams in hand; the master claimed it from the price of the slave; then the mukatab died from that illness without full payment; what the master received from the price of the slave is not secured for the master, even if the sale and receipt of price were in the presence of witnesses; so the thousand is reclaimed and given to the third party. The difference is that the loan scenario has obvious similarity, so the master is preferred, while in the sale scenario it is not obvious, so the third party is preferred, so reflect. And in it also: He wrote kitaba for his slave for two thousand, and he has two free sons, who are his heirs; the mukatab became ill and acknowledged to one of the sons one thousand dirhams, and acknowledged to the master a debt of one thousand dirhams; then he died.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter with several subsidiary rulings and disputes. It first clarifies the opposite scenario: when the slave's value exceeds the kitaba amount (e.g., kitaba 1000, value 2000), all scholars agree that the slave pays one-third of the value immediately or returns to slavery, as the concession (muhabah) is in both amount and deferment. Then from al-Muhit, a case where the kitaba is for the exact value: the slave must pay two-thirds immediately and one-third deferred. Then a ruling on acknowledging receipt of kitaba consideration in illness: if in health, acknowledgment is valid, but if in overwhelming debt, it is not. Then a will to write kitaba is valid. Then a dispute on manumitting a mukatab in illness: Abu Hanifa gives the slave an option to strive for two-thirds of his value or two-thirds of the kitaba debt, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say he strives for two-thirds of his value in one scenario, and in another for one-third. Then cases from al-Jami' involving competing debts and kitaba, where the master's claim may be preferred in loan cases but not in sale cases. The logic is that in loan the master has a direct claim, while in sale the third party's claim is stronger due to exchange. Finally, a case with a son of the mukatab and a complex division of assets. These examples illustrate how the one-third rule and principles of preference (tarjih) apply in marad al-mawt.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment of a debt or right; in illness, it is subject to the one-third rule if it harms heirs.
الثلث (al-thuluth): The one-third of an estate that a person can dispose of by bequest or gift in marad al-mawt without heirs' permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Muhit, if a sick master writes a kitaba for exactly the slave's value and dies with no other property, what must the slave do?
A) Pay the full amount immediately
B) Pay two-thirds of the consideration immediately and one-third deferred
C) Pay one-third immediately and the rest deferred
D) The kitaba is void and the slave returns to slavery unconditionally

Key Takeaway
The amount a mukatab must strive for when manumitted in marad al-mawt depends on whether the kitaba contract is considered surviving (Imam) or replaced by a bequest (Sahibayn).

Sa'y Calculation When a Master Frees a Mukatab in Terminal Illness
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sa'y calculation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi calculation for sa'y when a master frees a mukatab during terminal illness, distinguishing the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
When a terminally ill master frees a mukatab, sa'y is tied to value; Imam gives an option, Sahibayn limit it to value alone.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on how much a mukatab (slave under kitabah) must work (sa'y) when his master frees him during terminal illness or at death. The core question is whether sa'y is calculated based on the slave's market value or the amount owed under the kitabah contract. The disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) produces different practical outcomes, and the passage also covers partial payment scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three key scenarios
The passage covers three scenarios: (1) master writes kitabah in health, frees at death with no payment; (2) master receives a partial payment (500) then frees in illness and that payment is lost; (3) mukatab pays 100 then master frees in illness.
Focus on the first and third for clarity.
2
State the Sahibayn position for each scenario
In scenario 1, Sahibayn say sa'y is two-thirds of the slave's value (not the kitabah debt), and kitabah is void. In scenario 2, sa'y is one-third of value. In scenario 3, sa'y is two-thirds of the 100 (by consensus).
Sahibayn tie sa'y to value, not debt.
3
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position
In scenario 1, Imam gives the slave an option: work for two-thirds of value OR two-thirds of kitabah debt (i.e., 666.67 in the example). In scenario 2, the slave works for two-thirds of the original kitabah debt (which equals two-thirds of the remainder? The text indicates two-thirds of the original kitabah). In scenario 3, consensus with Sahibayn.
Imam's view provides flexibility.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
The difference is in the amount the slave must earn. In scenario 1, Sahibayn require 333.33 (two-thirds of 500), while Imam allows a choice of either 333.33 or 666.67. In scenario 2, Sahibayn require 166.67 (one-third of 500), Imam requires 666.67 (two-thirds of 1000). This affects the slave's burden.
The slave benefits from Imam's option in scenario 1 but is worse off in scenario 2.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Imam Abu Hanifa, as preferred by Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah and Ibn Nujaym in Al-Bahr al-Raiq. The mufta bihi is the Imam's option in scenario 1 and his calculation in scenario 2, though the partial payment scenario is less clear.
Authority: zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن كاتب عبده في مرضه ولا مال له غيره فأجازه الورثة في حياتهم فلهم الإبقاء بعد موته. ولو كاتب عبده في صحته على ألف وقيمته خمسمائة فأعتقه عند الموت ولم يقبض شيئاً حتى مات سعى في ثلثي قيمته عندهما وتبطل الكتابة. وقال الإمام: يسعى في ثلثي قیمته وإن شاء سعى في ثلثي ما عليه من الكتابة فإن قبض المولى خمسمائة ثم أعتقه في مرضه، فإن كان المقبوض هالكاً لم يحسب له شيء مما أدى وصار مال الكتابة ما بقي فيسعى في ثلثي كتابته لأن ثلثي كتابته وثلثي ما بقي من كتابته سواء. وعندهما يسعى في ثلث قيمته، ولو أدى المكاتب المائة أعتقه في مرضه سعى في ثلثي المائة بالإجماع اهـ.
And whoever writes a kitabah for his slave while terminally ill and has no other wealth, and the heirs approve during their lifetime, they have the right to keep [the arrangement] after his death. And if he wrote a kitabah for his slave while healthy for one thousand while his value is five hundred, and then he freed him at the time of death without having received anything until he died, he (the slave) works for two-thirds of his value according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and the kitabah is invalid. Imam Abu Hanifa said: He works for two-thirds of his value, and if he wishes, he works for two-thirds of what he owes from the kitabah. If the master received five hundred then freed him in his illness, if the received amount is lost, nothing is counted from what he paid, and the kitabah debt becomes the remainder, so he works for two-thirds of his kitabah because two-thirds of his kitabah and two-thirds of the remainder of his kitabah are the same. According to the two, he works for one-third of his value. And if the mukatab paid one hundred and he freed him in his illness, he works for two-thirds of the one hundred by consensus.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the calculation of what a mukatab (slave under kitabah) must pay (sa'y) when the master either dies or manumits him during terminal illness. The text first states the ruling of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): if the master, having written kitabah while healthy for an amount exceeding the slave's value, frees him at death without receiving payment, the slave works (sa'y) for two-thirds of his value (not the kitabah debt), and the kitabah is void. Imam Abu Hanifa gives the slave an option: either work for two-thirds of his value or two-thirds of the kitabah debt. Then another scenario: if the master had received a partial payment (500 out of 1000) then freed him in illness, and that payment is lost, the remaining kitabah debt is what the slave still owes. According to Imam Ahmad, the slave works for two-thirds of the original kitabah debt (which equals two-thirds of the remainder). The two say he works for one-third of his value. Finally, if the mukatab had paid 100 and the master freed him in illness, all agree (by consensus) that the slave works for two-thirds of that 100. The dispute turns on whether the sa'y is tied to the value or the kitabah price, and how partial payments affect the calculation.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a kitabah contract with his master, agreeing to pay a specified sum in exchange for freedom.
سعى (sa'a): To work and earn money to pay off the kitabah debt; legally, it refers to the obligation to earn and pay.
كتابة (kitabah): A contract of manumission by which a slave agrees to pay his master a sum in installments; upon full payment, the slave is free.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a master writes kitabah for his slave while healthy for 1000 dirhams, the slave's value is 500, and the master frees him at death without receiving anything, what do the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say the slave must pay?
A) Two-thirds of 1000 dirhams
B) Two-thirds of 500 dirhams
C) The full 1000 dirhams
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
The dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on sa'y calculation hinges on whether the obligation is tied to the slave's value or the kitabah debt; the mufta bihi is Imam's view giving the slave an option in the no-payment scenario.

Mukatab with Heirs' Disapproval: Immediate Payment Rule
Ruling on mukatab with heirs' disapproval

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a mukatab when the heirs do not approve the kitaba and calculate the amount due.

Core Idea
If heirs reject a mukatab contract, the slave must pay two-thirds of his value immediately or return to slavery.

This ruling consolidates a specific Hanafi case: a slave is contracted as a mukatab with a deferred payment, but the master dies before its completion. If the slave's value exceeds the kitaba amount and the heirs disapprove, the slave must immediately pay a proportional amount or revert to slavery. This ruling is based on consensus (ijma') and serves as a practical guideline for inheritance and manumission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Confirm that the master has died, the slave is a mukatab with a deferred payment, and the heirs have not approved the kitaba.
The deferred payment is set to a future date but not yet due.
2
Compare the slave's value to the kitaba amount
Determine the current market value of the slave at the master's death. In the given case, the value (2000) exceeds the kitaba (1000).
This ruling applies only when the value is greater than the kitaba.
3
Apply the rule for heirs' disapproval
When heirs disapprove, the slave must immediately pay two-thirds of his current value, not the kitaba amount.
Two-thirds is the portion of the slave's value that belongs to the heirs as inheritance; the master's share is one-third.
4
State the consequences
If the slave pays the two-thirds immediately, he becomes free. Otherwise, he reverts to being a slave owned by the heirs.
The payment is an immediate obligation (wajib).

Source Text

رقيقاً وإن كاتبه على ألف إلى سنة وقيمته ألفان ولم تجز الورثة أدى ثلثي القيمة حالاً وإلا رد رقيقاً. وهذا بالإجماع وقد تقدم بيانه.
As a slave: if he made a kitaba with him for a thousand to be paid in a year, and his value is two thousand, and the heirs did not approve, he pays two-thirds of the value immediately, otherwise he is returned as a slave. This is by consensus and its explanation has preceded.

The case: a slave is contracted as a mukatab for 1,000 dirhams payable in one year, but at the time of the master's death, the slave is worth 2,000 dirhams. The heirs do not approve the kitaba. The ruling: the slave must pay two-thirds of his current value (i.e., 1,333.33 dirhams) immediately; failing that, he reverts to being a slave. This is based on consensus (ijma') as the author notes. The detailed proof was given earlier in the text.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master for his freedom upon payment of a specified sum.
كتابة (kitaba): Contract between master and slave for manumission in exchange for payment.

Joint Kitaba for Present and Absent Slave
Expansion of matn ruling on joint kitaba with absent slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on joint kitaba for a present and absent slave, explain the istihsan justification, and apply the follower (taba‘iyya) principle to similar cases.

Core Idea
In joint kitaba, the absent slave follows the present; istihsan overrides qiyas.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq expands on a matn from Kanz al-Daqa’iq regarding a joint kitaba contract for two slaves—one present, one absent. The matn rules that if the present slave accepts, the contract is valid. The commentary explores why this is not simply a suspended contract, but valid immediately by istihsan, based on the follower principle. By studying this, you will understand how Hanafi fiqh uses istihsan to facilitate transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and scenario
The matn states that writing kitaba for present and absent, with acceptance by the present, is valid. The scenario: the present slave proposes the kitaba including himself and the absent, and the master accepts.
The acceptance is only from the present slave; the absent is not required to accept.
2
Understand the qiyas objection
By qiyas, the kitaba would be effective only for the present slave, and the absent's part would be suspended pending his acceptance, akin to selling another's property.
Qiyas would require the absent's consent to be bound.
3
Analyse the istihsan justification
The master addresses the present slave intentionally and makes the absent a follower (tabi‘). This is analogous to an umm walad's kitaba including her children as followers, and to a conditional freeing (ta‘liq) by the master on his own slave, which requires no acceptance.
Istihsan prioritises practical facilitation over strict analogy.
4
Apply the follower principle to sub-rulings
The present is solely obligated to pay the full amount; the absent owes nothing. If the present pays, both become free. If the absent earns, the master cannot take it. Absolving the present frees both; absolving the absent has no effect.
The absent's followership means he has no direct obligation.
5
Note practical consequences
If the master frees the absent, the present's share reduces. If the present fails, the absent may be sold. Whichever pays the full amount is freed. The joint kitaba is valid immediately, not suspended.
This ruling allows an efficient path to freedom for both slaves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن كاتب الحاضر والغائب وقبل الحاضر صح) يعني إذا كاتب عبدين أحدهما حاضر والآخر غائب بأن قال العبد لمولاه كاتبني بألف عن نفسي وعن فلان الغائب فكاتبهما فقبل الحاضر جاز. وفي المحيط : ولو كاتب عبداً حاضراً وآخر غائباً وقبل الحاضر جاز استحساناً .اهـ. فظهر أنه لا فرق في البداية بين أن تكون من السيد أو من العبد. والقياس أن الحاضر مكاتباً وحده لأنه عقد الكتابة على نفسه وعلى الغائب فينفذ عليه يصير ويتوقف في حق الغائب على أجازته كما إذا باع ماله ومال غيره أو كاتب عبده وعبد غيره، وجه الاستحسان أن المولى خاطب الحاضر قصداً وجعل الغائب تبعاً له والكتابة على هذا الوجه مشروعة كالأمة إذا كوتبت دخل في كتابتها ولدها المولود في الكتابة أو المشتري فيها أو المضموم إليها في العقد تبعاً لها حتى يعتقوا بأدائها وليس عليهم شيء من البدل، ولأن هذا تعليق العتق بأداء الحاضر والمولى ينفرد به في حق الغائب فينفذ من غير توقف ولا قبول من الغائب كما لو كاتب الحاضر بألف ثم قال إن أديته إلي ففلان حر فإنه يصح من غير قبول الحاضر فكذا هذا، فإذا أمكن جعل الغائب تبعاً استغنى عن شرط رضاه وینفرد به الحاضر ويطالب الحاضر بكل البدل ولا عبرة بإجازة الغائب ولا رده ولا يؤاخذ الغائب بالبدل ولا بشيء منه . ولو اكتسب شيئاً ليس للمولى أن يأخذه من يده ولو أبرأه المولى أو وهب له مال الكتابة لا يصح لعدم وجوبه عليه، ولو أبرأ الحاضر أو وهبه مال الكتابة عتقا، ولو أعتق الغائب سقط عن الحاضر حصته بخلاف الولد المولود في الكتابة حيث لا يسقط عن الأم شيء من البدل بعتقه وكذا ولدها المشتري. ولو أعتق الحاضر لم يعتق الغائب وسقط عن الحاضر حصته من البدل ويؤدي الغائب حصته حالاً أو يرد رقيقاً لأن الأجل لم يثبت في حق الغائب. وفي المحيط : وإن مات الغائب لم يدفع عن الحاضر شيء. وذكر عصام لا يبيع الغائب ما لم يعجز الحاضر .اهـ. قال رحمه الله تعالى: (وأيهما أدى عتقا) أي أيهما أدى بدل
He (the author) said: 'And if he wrote a kitaba for the present and the absent, and the present accepted, it is valid.' Meaning, if he wrote a kitaba for two slaves, one present and one absent, such that the slave said to his master: 'Write a kitaba for me for a thousand on behalf of myself and on behalf of so-and-so the absent,' and he wrote a kitaba for them, and the present accepted, it is valid. In al-Muhit: 'And if he wrote a kitaba for a present slave and an absent slave, and the present accepted, it is valid by istihsan.' So it is clear that there is no difference in the beginning whether it is from the master or from the slave. The qiyas is that the present slave becomes a mukatab alone because he made the contract of kitaba on himself and on the absent; it is effective on him and becomes, and is suspended regarding the absent pending his acceptance, just as if he sold his property and the property of another, or wrote a kitaba for his slave and another's slave. The basis of istihsan is that the master addressed the present slave intentionally and made the absent a follower of him, and kitaba in this manner is legislated, like an umm walad (slave mother) when she is given kitaba; her child born during the kitaba enters her kitaba, or the purchased child, or the one added to her in the contract as a follower, so that they are freed by her payment and nothing of the payment is on them. And because this is a ta'liq (suspension) of freedom upon the payment of the present, and the master alone does that regarding the absent, so it is executed without suspension or acceptance from the absent, just as if he wrote a kitaba for the present for a thousand and then said: 'If you pay it to me, so-and-so is free,' it is valid without acceptance from the present. So similarly here. So if it is possible to make the absent a follower, there is no need for the condition of his consent, and the present alone is obligated, and he is required to pay the entire payment, and no attention is paid to the acceptance or rejection of the absent, nor is the absent required to pay anything of the payment. And if he earns something, the master may not take it from his hand. And if the master absolves him or gifts him the kitaba property, it is not valid because it is not obligatory on him. And if the master absolves the present or gifts him the kitaba property, they are both freed. And if he frees the absent, his share falls from the present, unlike a child born during the kitaba, where nothing of the payment falls from the mother by his freeing, and similarly her purchased child. And if he frees the present, the absent is not freed, and the present's share of the payment falls, but the absent pays his share immediately or is returned as a slave because the term was not established regarding the absent. In al-Muhit: 'And if the absent dies, nothing is reduced from the present.' And Isam mentioned: 'The absent is not sold unless the present becomes unable to pay.' He (the author) said: 'And whichever of them pays, he is freed.'
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This is an expansion probe on a matn ruling about writing a joint kitaba for a present and an absent slave. The matn states: 'If he wrote a kitaba for the present and the absent, and the present accepted, it is valid.' The commentary clarifies the scenario: the slave (present) asks the master to write a kitaba for him on behalf of himself and another absent slave. The master does so, and the present slave accepts. The ruling is valid by istihsan, even though by qiyas one would say only the present becomes a mukatab and the absent's part is suspended pending his acceptance. The basis of istihsan is that the master intended the present slave and made the absent a follower (tabi'). This is similar to the case of an umm walad where her children born during kitaba are included as followers. Also, it is like a ta'liq: the master can condition the freeing of the absent upon the present's payment, which is valid without the absent's acceptance. Therefore, the present slave is obligated to pay the entire sum, and the absent is not required to pay anything. The text then discusses sub-rulings: if the absent earns something, the master cannot take it; if the master absolves the absent, it has no effect because nothing is owed from him; but if he absolves the present, the present is freed; if the master frees the absent, the present's share decreases; but the opposite is not true: freeing the present does not free the absent, and the absent must then either pay his share immediately or be enslaved. The absent is not sold unless the present fails to pay. Finally, the matn continues: 'And whichever of them pays, he is freed.' Meaning either the present or the absent may pay the full amount, and whichever does so is freed. This is consistent with the follower principle.

Key Terms
تبع (tabi'): Follower; the absent slave is treated as a follower of the present slave in the kitaba contract.
أمة (umm walad): A slave mother who has borne her master's child; here used as analogy for including her offspring in her kitaba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a joint kitaba for a present and absent slave, if the present slave pays the full amount, what happens to the absent slave?
A) He becomes free immediately
B) He remains a slave unless he pays his share
C) He becomes free only if the present slave also pays for him
D) He becomes free only if he consents

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, joint kitaba for present and absent is valid by istihsan: the absent slave is a follower, his separate consent is not required, and payment by either frees both.

Joint Kitaba for a Present and Absent Slave: Istihsan and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains the validity and consequences of a joint kitaba for a present and absent slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a joint kitaba for a present and absent slave, explain the istihsan reasoning, and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding subsequent approval of payment.

Core Idea
A joint kitaba is valid by istihsan; the absent slave is a follower, and his consent is not required.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq addresses a specific scenario: a master writes a kitaba contract for two slaves—one present, one absent. The present slave accepts. Is the contract valid? By strict qiyas, it would be suspended on the absent’s approval. However, the Hanafi school applies istihsan, treating the absent as a follower, thus validating the contract immediately. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and also examines a related dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on whether the master’s subsequent approval of payment can cure a defective payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a joint kitaba (contract of manumission in exchange for payment) for two slaves, one present and one absent, with the present slave accepting. The ruling is that the contract is valid (sahih) by istihsan, despite the absent slave not accepting.
By qiyas, it would be suspended; istihsan deviates based on the principle of making the absent a follower.
2
Understand the qiyas objection
Analogy to selling one’s own property along with another’s: valid only for one’s own share; the other’s share is suspended on approval. Similarly, the kitaba should be valid only for the present slave and suspended for the absent.
The illa (effective cause) is the inclusion of another’s liability without consent.
3
Grasp the istihsan justification
The master intentionally addressed the present slave and made the absent a follower (tabi‘). This is analogous to a female mukataba whose children enter as followers. Thus, the absent’s consent is unnecessary, and the present slave is solely liable for the entire exchange.
The follower principle allows the contract to be executed without the absent’s acceptance.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on subsequent approval
If a third party paid on behalf of the absent slave without prior approval, and the master later approves both the kitaba and the payment, Abu Yusuf considers the payment valid and the slave free. Abu Hanifa does not accept retrospective approval of the payment alone.
This affects the status of the slave’s earnings before approval.
5
Apply the ruling to consequences
Whichever slave pays the exchange is freed; the paying slave cannot seek reimbursement from the other. If the master frees the present, the absent must pay his share or revert. If the master frees the absent, the present’s share is reduced.
The absent’s earnings before payment cannot be taken by the master.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كاتب الحاضر والغائب وقبل الحاضر صح وأيهما أدى عتقا ولا يرجع على صاحبه كان وهب له الألف ثم بلغ المولى فأجاز فالهبة باطلة ولو أن رجلاً كاتب عبد الغير بغير إذنه على ألف درهم فأدى العبد الألف إليه ثم بلغ المولى فأجاز الكتابة جازت الكتابة ولا يجوز الدفع ولا يعتق بذلك الدفع، فإن أجاز المولى الكتابة والدفع فذلك جائز في قول أبي يوسف ويعتق المكاتب بدفعه، ولا تجوز إجازة القبض في قول الإمام وما اكتسبه بعد الكتابة قبل الإجازة فذلك للمكاتب على كل حال .اهـ. وفي شرح الطحاوي ولو كان لرجل عبد غائب فخاطب رجل مولاه فقال كاتب عبدك الغائب على ألف فهذا على وجهين : إما أن يشترط الضمان أو لم يشترط أما إذا لم يضمن فالكتابة جائزة ويتوقف على إجازة العبد، فإن أجازه جاز ولزمه الألف، وإن رده بطل، فلو أن هذا الرجل أدى قبل أن يجيز العبد وقبل أن يفسخ جاز وعتق العبد، وليس له أن يسترد ذلك في الاستحسان اهـ . قال رحمه الله : (وإن كاتب الحاضر والغائب وقبل الحاضر صح) يعني إذا كاتب عبدين أحدهما حاضر والآخر غائب بأن قال العبد لمولاه كاتبني بألف عن نفسي وعن فلان الغائب فكاتبهما فقبل الحاضر جاز. وفي المحيط : ولو كاتب عبداً حاضراً وآخر غائباً وقبل الحاضر جاز استحساناً .اهـ. فظهر أنه لا فرق في البداية بين أن تكون من السيد أو من العبد. والقياس أن الحاضر مكاتباً وحده لأنه عقد الكتابة على نفسه وعلى الغائب فينفذ عليه يصير ويتوقف في حق الغائب على أجازته كما إذا باع ماله ومال غيره أو كاتب عبده وعبد غيره، وجه الاستحسان أن المولى خاطب الحاضر قصداً وجعل الغائب تبعاً له والكتابة على هذا الوجه مشروعة كالأمة إذا كوتبت دخل في كتابتها ولدها المولود في الكتابة أو المشتري فيها أو المضموم إليها في العقد تبعاً لها حتى يعتقوا بأدائها وليس عليهم شيء من البدل، ولأن هذا تعليق العتق بأداء الحاضر والمولى ينفرد به في حق الغائب فينفذ من غير توقف ولا قبول من الغائب كما لو كاتب الحاضر بألف ثم قال إن أديته إلي ففلان حر فإنه يصح من غير قبول الحاضر فكذا هذا، فإذا أمكن جعل الغائب تبعاً استغنى عن شرط رضاه وینفرد به الحاضر ويطالب الحاضر بكل البدل ولا عبرة بإجازة الغائب ولا رده ولا يؤاخذ الغائب بالبدل ولا بشيء منه . ولو اكتسب شيئاً ليس للمولى أن يأخذه من يده ولو أبرأه المولى أو وهب له مال الكتابة لا يصح لعدم وجوبه عليه، ولو أبرأ الحاضر أو وهبه مال الكتابة عتقا، ولو أعتق الغائب سقط عن الحاضر حصته بخلاف الولد المولود في الكتابة حيث لا يسقط عن الأم شيء من البدل بعتقه وكذا ولدها المشتري. ولو أعتق الحاضر لم يعتق الغائب وسقط عن الحاضر حصته من البدل ويؤدي الغائب حصته حالاً أو يرد رقيقاً لأن الأجل لم يثبت في حق الغائب. وفي المحيط : وإن مات الغائب لم يدفع عن الحاضر شيء. وذكر عصام لا يبيع الغائب ما لم يعجز الحاضر .اهـ. قال رحمه الله تعالى: (وأيهما أدى عتقا) أي أيهما أدى بدل بشيء ولا يؤاخذ الغائب بشيء وقبوله لغو
He wrote for the present and the absent [slave], and the present accepted, it is valid. Whichever of them pays the exchange is freed, and he does not seek recourse from his companion for anything. If he had gifted him the thousand, then the master came to know and approved, the gift is void. If a man wrote a kitaba for another's slave without his permission for a thousand dirhams, and the slave paid the thousand to him, then the master came to know and approved the kitaba, the kitaba is valid, but the payment is not valid and he is not freed by that payment. If the master approved both the kitaba and the payment, that is permissible in the opinion of Abu Yusuf, and the mukatab is freed by his payment. But approval of the receipt is not permissible in the opinion of Imam [Abu Hanifa]. Whatever he earns after the kitaba before the approval belongs to the mukatab in any case. End. In Sharh al-Tahawi: If a man has an absent slave, and a man addressed his master, saying: 'Write a kitaba for your absent slave for a thousand,' this is in two aspects: either he stipulates guarantee or he does not. If he does not guarantee, the kitaba is valid and is suspended on the slave's approval. If he approves it, it is valid and the thousand is binding on him. If he rejects it, it becomes void. If this man paid before the slave approved and before he rescinded, it is valid and the slave is freed, and he has no right to reclaim it, by istihsan. End. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (If he writes a kitaba for the present and the absent, and the present accepts, it is valid). Meaning: if he writes a kitaba for two slaves, one present and one absent, by the slave saying to his master: 'Write a kitaba for me for a thousand on behalf of myself and on behalf of so-and-so the absent,' so he writes a kitaba for them both, and the present accepts, it is valid. In al-Muhit: If he writes a kitaba for a present slave and an absent one, and the present accepts, it is valid by istihsan. End. So it appears that there is no difference initially whether it is from the master or from the slave. By qiyas, the present slave becomes a mukatab alone, because he concluded the kitaba contract on himself and on the absent one, so it is executed on him and becomes effective, but it is suspended in the right of the absent on his approval, as if he sold his property and the property of another, or wrote a kitaba for his slave and the slave of another. The istihsan justification: the master addressed the present intentionally and made the absent a follower of him. And kitaba in this manner is legislated, like when a female slave is given kitaba, her child born during the kitaba or purchased [as part of the contract] or the one included in the contract enters as a follower of her, so they are freed by her payment, and nothing of the exchange is upon them. And because this is a suspension of freedom upon the payment of the present, and the master alone [acts] in the right of the absent, so it is executed without suspension or acceptance from the absent, as if he wrote a kitaba for the present for a thousand and then said: 'If you pay it to me, so-and-so is free,' then it is valid without acceptance from the present. So too this. When it is possible to make the absent a follower, the condition of his consent is dispensed with, and the present alone is [liable]. The present is demanded for the entire exchange. There is no consideration for the approval or rejection of the absent. The absent is not taken to account for the exchange or anything of it. If he earns something, the master has no right to take it from his hand. If the master declares him free of debt or gifts him the property of the kitaba, it is not valid due to lack of obligation upon him. If he declares the present free of debt or gifts him the property of the kitaba, they are freed. If he frees the absent, the present's share falls from him, unlike the child born during the kitaba, where nothing of the exchange falls from the mother by his freeing, and likewise her purchased child. If he frees the present, the absent is not freed, and the present's share of the exchange falls from him, and the absent pays his share immediately or returns as a slave, because the term was not established in the right of the absent. In al-Muhit: If the absent dies, nothing is reduced from the present. 'Isam mentioned: the absent is not sold as long as the present has not become incapable. End. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (Whichever of them pays, they are freed) meaning whichever of them pays the exchange ... something, and the absent is not taken to account for anything, and his acceptance is void.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage combines a ruling statement with an intra-Hanafi dispute and an istihsan-based expansion. The main ruling is that if a master contracts kitaba (a contract of manumission in exchange for payment) for two slaves, one present and one absent, and the present slave accepts, the contract is valid by istihsan (juristic preference). The present slave becomes a mukatab on behalf of both, and the absent slave is considered a follower (tabi'). Consequently, the acceptance or rejection of the absent slave is irrelevant. Whichever slave pays the full exchange (al-badal) is freed, but the paying slave cannot seek reimbursement from the other because the payment is either a fulfillment of his own obligation (if the present pays) or a voluntary act without mandate (if the absent pays). The passage also discusses a related dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf: if a third party pays the exchange on behalf of the absent slave without the master's prior approval, Abu Yusuf considers the payment valid if the master subsequently approves both the kitaba and the payment, while Abu Hanifa does not accept retrospective approval of the receipt. The commentary elaborates on the istihsan reasoning by analogy to a female mukataba whose children enter as followers, and to a conditional promise of freeing a third party upon the mukatab's payment. The practical consequence is that the present slave is solely liable for the entire exchange, and the absent slave has no obligation; if the present slave earns extra income, the master cannot take it; if the master forgives the debt or gifts the property of kitaba to the present, both are freed; if the master frees the absent, the present's share is reduced; if the present is freed, the absent must pay his share immediately or revert to slavery.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a kitaba contract with his master, agreeing to pay a specified sum in exchange for freedom upon full payment.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that deviates from qiyas (analogy) based on a stronger evidence, custom, or necessity.
تابع (tabi'): A follower who enters a contract incidentally, without being a primary party; his rights and obligations are derivative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a third party pays the exchange on behalf of an absent slave without prior approval, and the master later approves both the kitaba and the payment, what is the ruling?
A) The payment is valid and the absent slave is freed per Abu Yusuf.
B) The payment is invalid and the absent slave remains a slave per Abu Hanifa.
C) Both views are equally accepted; mufta bihi is to follow Abu Yusuf.
D) The kitaba itself becomes void.

Key Takeaway
A joint kitaba for a present and absent slave is valid by istihsan; the absent is a follower, and the present alone is liable for the exchange.

Kitaba for a Female Slave and Her Young Sons
Ruling statement with proof by istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a female slave contracting kitaba for herself and her young sons, and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas in this case.

Core Idea
A female slave's kitaba for herself and her young sons is valid by stipulation, not guardianship.

This lesson examines the validity of a female slave contracting a manumission agreement (kitaba) not only for herself but also for her young sons. The Hanafi school, through istihsan, upholds the contract as valid even though the mother lacks legal guardianship over her children. The key reasoning is that the children enter as followers by stipulation, analogous to an absent slave included in a kitaba. Understanding this ruling clarifies the Hanafi application of istihsan in cases where strict qiyas would yield injustice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a female slave writing kitaba for herself and her two young sons. The Hanafi ruling is sahih (valid) by istihsan.
2
Understand the qiyas objection
By strict analogy (qiyas), the contract would be invalid because a slave mother has no legal guardianship (wilaya) over her children to bind them to a contract.
Qiyas is set aside by istihsan due to the need for ease.
3
Trace the istihsan argument
The istihsan is based on analogy to the case of including an absent slave in the kitaba of a present slave. Here, the children enter by stipulation (shart) not by guardianship, making them followers (tabi').
The acceptance or rejection by the children is irrelevant.
4
Note the practical consequence
If any one of them (mother or son) pays the entire exchange, everyone is freed and the payer has no recourse against the others. The child's earnings belong to himself, and if killed, blood money goes to the parents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كاتب الأمة عن نفسها وعن ابنين صغيرين لها صح وأي أدى لم يرجع . وهذا استحسان، والقياس أن لا يجوز وقد ذكرنا وجهه في مسألة الغائب لأن هذه مثلها في جميع ما ذكرنا من الأحكام لما أن الأم والأب الرقيق لا ولاية له على ولده فيكون دخول الولد في كتابتهما بالشرط لا بالولاية كدخول الغائب في كتابة الحاضر وقبول الأولاد وردهم وفي المحيط : كاتب عبده وامرأته على أنفسهما وأولادهما الصغار، ثم إن إنساناً قتل الولد فقيمته للأبوين ولو غاب الأب فأراد المولى استسعاء الولد في شيء من الكتابة لم يكن له ذلك ولا سبيل للأبوين على كسب الولد لأنه مكاتب أصلاً بخلاف المولود في الكتابة لأنه دخل تبعاً فكان كسبه تبعاً. ويدفع حصته عن الأبوين إن أعتقه السيد، وإن مات الأبوين أدى حالاً يعتبر .
And if a female slave writes a kitaba for herself and for her two young sons, it is valid. Whichever of them pays does not seek recourse [from the others]. And this is istihsan. By qiyas, it is not valid, and we have mentioned its justification in the issue of the absent [slave], because this is like it in all the rulings we mentioned, due to the fact that the mother and father who are slaves have no guardianship over their child. So the inclusion of the child in their kitaba is by stipulation, not by guardianship, like the inclusion of the absent in the kitaba of the present, and the acceptance and rejection of the children [are irrelevant]. In al-Muhit: He wrote a kitaba for his slave and his wife on their own behalf and on behalf of their young children. Then if a person kills the child, his value is for the parents. If the father is absent and the master wants to employ the child in earning something of the kitaba, he has no right to do so, nor do the parents have any access to the child's earnings, because he is a mukatab from the beginning, unlike the child born during the kitaba, because he entered as a follower, so his earnings are also follow [i.e., belong to the mother's kitaba]. And he [the child] pays his share on behalf of the parents if the master frees him. If the parents die, he pays immediately. Considered.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with a proof by istihsan. It addresses the case of a female slave (umm walad or ordinary slave) who contracts kitaba for herself and her two young sons. The ruling states that the contract is valid (sahih) by way of istihsan. Qiyas would hold it invalid because the mother, being a slave, has no legal guardianship (wilaya) over her children to bind them to a contract. However, istihsan prevails by analogy to the earlier case of the present and absent slave: the children enter as followers (tabi') of the mother, just as the absent slave was a follower of the present. Since the inclusion is by stipulation (shart) and not by guardianship, it is permissible. Consequently, if any one of them (mother or son) pays the entire exchange, everyone is freed, and the payer cannot seek recourse from the others. The commentary also discusses a scenario from al-Muhit: if a third party kills the son during the kitaba, the blood money goes to the parents (as heirs) because the child is a mukatab and his earnings belong to him. The master cannot force the child to earn for the kitaba; the child's earnings are his own, unlike a child born during the mother's kitaba, whose earnings are attributed to the mother's kitaba. If the master frees the child, the child must pay his share of the exchange on behalf of the parents. If the parents die, the child must pay his share immediately (halaman).

Key Terms
ولاية (wilaya): Legal authority or guardianship to act on behalf of another, especially in contracts.
شرط (shart): A stipulation in the contract that includes the children; it is the basis for their inclusion, not guardianship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the kitaba of a female slave for herself and her young sons valid by istihsan despite the mother having no guardianship?
A) Because the children are considered property of the mother.
B) Because the children enter as followers by stipulation, analogous to an absent slave in a kitaba.
C) Because the master gives permission for the children.
D) Because the children have independent legal capacity.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a female slave's kitaba for her young sons by istihsan, as the children are included by stipulation as followers, not by guardianship.
